Creegan are guilty of forgery they should be punished
for it. Becker and Creegan do not share this view,
and the question to be adjudicated is as to whether
they know more than the courts.
It may be true that the Kaiser has assisted in fight
ing a fire, but he had no ax nor red-leather helmet.
He needn't try to pose as a Jim Budd.
There might be meaner things said about Alger.
He has not yet been accused of mailing an infernal
machine to Miles.

should he not pair with J. Aubrey Jones and both
stay at home?

out of the war was a title and a coat of tan, and the tan
will wear off.

ODDS AND ENDS.
Ophthalmaloglcal statistlca, it is said,
show that Spain has more blind men than
any other nation in western Europe.
It is not generally known that Napoleon
111 was one of the earliest advocates of
a Panama canal. He wrote much on the
subject when imprisoned at Ham.
A man pressing upon a dynamometer
with one hand exerts a force equal to
three-quarters of his own weight and a
dog lifting his lower Jaw does it with a
force equal to eight times his own weight.
But crabs and mussels are even more
muscular. The pinch of a crab Is no jok
ing matter, considering that it is effected
with a force equal to twenty-eight times
its own weight. Worse, still is it to be
caught between the shells of a mussel. A
mussel closes its shell with a force equal
to 382 times its own weight.

ST. PATRICK'S SEMINARY AT MENLO PARK DEDICATED.
Services Conducted Under the Direction of Archbishop Riordanâ Opening of the Institution.
St. Patrick's Seminary, the new Catholic theological college at Menlo Park, was dedicated Wednesday morn-
Ing with solemn and impressive ceremony. $
With its dedication has come the crowning of the one cherished hope and the life object of Archbishop Rior
dan, its founder, who hns labored for years in planning and realizing its institution as a means of educating
young men of the State who are desirous of embracing the ministry of the Catholic church. Robed in the sacred
vestments of the altar, assisted by clergymen from all parts of the State and members of the Franciscan. Salesian,
Marist. Jesuit and Sulpician orders, the Archbishop conducted the ceremony of dedication in
person, piving to the Catholic church, as the sacred words were pronounced, one of the largest and
one of the most perfect theological seminaries in the country.
Immediately after the banquet which was tendered at the close of the religious exercises the Archbishop gave
a hrW'f and eloquent address on the object of the institution, its aim and his hopes as a founder for its future.
Ho was followed in the address by Bishop Montgomery of Los Angeles and Bishop Grace of Sacramento, who pro
nounced a Joint eulogium on his efforts in realizing the consummation of his cherished plan in giving to the State
and to the church the institution which now claims him as its founder and guardiun.
Rev. A. J. B. Vuibert. S. S. . together with a number of other members of the Sulpician Order, were installed
by the approbation of the Archbishop, immediately after the banquet., as the faculty of the new seminary. To them
is* Invested the trust and the confidence to properly guide and educate the young men who are sent, and will be
sent, under their care for thorough preparation as ministers of the gospel and of God.
â With the choosing of Father Vuibert, the Archbishop has chosen a member of an order famed for its educa
tors and renowned for its history as an educational community in the Catholic church.
The order, which takes its name from Its founder, St. Sulpice, was first organized in the sixteenth century, and
has since flourished as one of the greatest educational bodies in the teaching of theology in the church. For 200
years it has been in existence In Montreal, and for over a century has prepared, educated and watched over the
destiny of students that have matriculated during this time In the college at Baltimore, the greatest theological
seminary in the United States and, perhaps, greatest in the world.
The time scheduled for the formal opening of the seminary is September 20 next, and already there are nearly
forty applications for entrance from young men from various parts of the State.
Those entering the college will have to undergo a thorough preparatory course which will extend over a period
of five years. Upon graduating from this school of fundamental preparation they are then eligible for enroll
ment in the theological seminary proper, where they will undergo a course of study of seven years' duration.
The architecture of the new edifice conforms rigidly to the Romanesque style with all its beauties and impres
slveness. The main entrance, situated in the west wing, the administration building and the annex, are already
completed and it is anticipated that the other unfinished portions of the building, as yet, will be completed within
the near future and before the time indicated for the formal opening of the seminary. All through the entire
building the floors are of polished inlaid woods with a similar effect in wainscoting and ceiling decoration.
On the main floor are the living rooms of the professors, reception rooms for guests and various other apart
ments. The other floors of the edifice are divided off and devoted to the various purposes for which they
have been designed in suiting the comfort and convenience of the students, the faculty and visitors.
Among those who attended the ceremony of dedication were the following: Archbishop Riordan, Bishop Mont
gomery of Monterey and Los Angeles, Bishop Grace of Sacramento, Rev. Peter Yorke, chancellor; Rev. P. E. Mulli
gan, secretary of the Archbishop; Very Rev. J. J. Prendergast, vicar general: Rev. Edward Dempsey, assistant
pastor St. Mary's Cathedral; Rev. C. Ramm. St. Mary's Cathedral: Rev. Philip O'Ryan. St. Mary's Cathedral; Rev.
M. D, Connolly, Rev. P. Cummins, St. Charles Borromeo; Father Glubert, Marist, French Church; Father Dubois,
Marist, French Church: Rev. J. B. McNally, Rev. J. M. Casln. Of the Jesuit order: Very Rev. J. Frieden. S. J.,
president St. Ignatius College; Rev. J. W. Riordan. S. J. ; Rev. G. Leggto, S. J.; Rev. Hugh Gallagher, S. J.; Rev.
William Melchers. S. J.; Rev. A. Mazzetti, 8. J. ; Rev. V. Testa, S. J.; Rev. C. Franchi. S. J. Of the Franciscan or
der: Very Rev. Maximilian Neuman, superior of St. Boniface; Father Augustine, O. F. M. ; Father Gregory, O. F.
M, ; Father Raphael. O. F. M; Rev. J. J. Cranwell, chaplain of St. Mary's College; Rev. M. K<noally, St. Rose's;
Rev. J. M. Gleason. St. Rose's; Brother Florlnus; Rev. J. F. McGinty. Holy Cross: Rev. T. Callaghan, St. Mat
thew'?. San Mateo; Rev. B. ReJahan. Salesian' Order: Rev. R. E. Kenna, S. J., San Jose; Rev. P. A Fole>, Brother
Michael, Father Phelan. Brother Bettelin, Rev. J. Conlan. Rev. J. McQuaide. Holy Cross; Father Jacob!. S. J. â¢

Want Their Money Eack.
C. S. Olsen. as assignee of fourteen gold
seekers, has sued the Yukon and North
western Dredging and Transportation
Company to recover $7300 alleged to have
been paid to the company for transpor
tation to the Klondike. The defendant
agreed, it Is alleged, to transport each of
the claimants to Dawson City on the
steamer City of Dawson, which was to
leave this city not later than June 1 of
the present year. The fares for transpor
tation have been paid in to the company,
but up to date the defendant corporation
has made no endeavor to carry out its
contract. Hence the suit.

FRAMES A CONSTITUTION.
Central American Commission Com
pletes Its Labors.
MANAGUA, Nicaragua, Aug. 25 (via
Galveston).â The convention which has
been engaged in formulating a constitu
tion for the use of Central America, em
bracing Salvador, Honduras and Nicar
agua, has concluded Its work. The Com
missioners were appointed to convene at
Amapala, Honduras, on the first of No
vember next. These Commissioners will
supplant the Diet of the Greater Repub
lic of Central America, and will assume
provisional federal powers. They will
make preparations for the election on
December 13 for a President, Senators.
Representatives and Federal Judges for
terms of four years, and for the Instal
lation of those officials elected on March
1. 1599. The Federal district includes more
than 0000 square miles on the Pacific
Coast. The constieution has not been sub-
mitted to a vote of the people.
ADVOCATING ANNEXATION.
Jamaicans Prefer Uncle Sam's Pro
tection to Ruin.
KINGSTON. Jamaica, Aug. 25.â The
American movement for annexation com
menced on Monday at Manchester and
rapidly spread and Is finding general ac
ceptance as the only visible alternative
to ruin. The promoters of the move
ment are pushing it vigorously, being de
sirous of strengthening the hands of Ja
maica's representative at the Barbadoes
conference, thus encouraging intercolon
ial co-operation. The sole obstacle en
countered is the color question.

THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, FRIDAY, AUGUST 26, 1898.
RYTHIAHS WILL
CUT EXPENSES
Supreme Lodge Wield
ing an Ax.
BIG SLASHING OF SALARIES
MILEAGE REDUCED TO ITS AC
TUAL COST.
Committee Report Starts a Fight
That Has Almost Become Per
sonal Among Some o f
the Members.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
INDIANAPOLIS, Ind., Aug. 25.â The
election of the officers of the Supreme
Lodge. Knights of Pythias, did not
take place this morning. It Is usual
to elect officers on the third day of the
biennial session, but it was occupied
by investigations that are going on.
There were stormy times in the lodge
rooms this morning. Appointments
which usually went through with a
whirl at former meetings were either
referred or voted down, and there was
a strong determination on the part of
those opposed to the present supreme
officers to track close to Pythian laws
in everything. This morning a resolu
tion was introduced that will cut the
expenses of the meeting of the Su
preme Lodge in two, and will make
this session the cheapest ever held with
so many representatives.
A resolution was passed requiring
reports from the officers as to sup
plies furnished and declaring that in
the future such purchases must be
made by competitive bids.
The sensation this afternoon was the
report of the estimate of the finance
committee. The committee went at
thr- salaries with an ax and choDDed
everything in sight. It recommended
that the per diem of the supreme repre
sentatives be reduced from $5 per day
and 5 cents a mile each way to $3 a
day and 3 cents a mile actually trav
eled. The report as presented is as
follows:
Salary of supreme chancellor, two
years, $6000: salary of clerical force. two
years. $1800; office rent, at actual cost
and not to exceed, for two yearÃ. $800.
Postage, telephone, telegrams, sta
tionery, express, removal of furniture
from Hastings, Mich., all at actual cost
not to exceed, for two years, $1000.
Salary of supreme keeper of records,
two years, $7200; clerical assistance of
all kinds, two years. $2000; office rent,
heating, lighting, Janitor, telephone,
postage, packing boxes, wrapping paper
and other incidentals, at actual cost,
not to exceed $2000; traveling expense
St X R and S., at actual rate cost and
not to exceed, two years. $300.
Salary of S. M. of Ex.. two years,
$1500; traveling expenses, at actual cost
and not to exceed $400.
Expense of the Supreme Tribunal,
two years, $3000; official records, at
actual coot and not to exceed $3000: al
lowance to daughters of founders.
$1400; traveling expenses of the finance
re- -iinittee, $1200; interest on mortgagps,
$1350; Supreme Chancellor's jewel, $150.
Total. $33,500.
This report has precipitated a fight
that has almost become personal be
tween some of the members.
The parade of the Knights of Khor
assan took place to-night and was wit
nessed by thousands upon thousands
of people. It was the greatest gather
ing of people attracted down town any
evening during the encampment. About
2000 Knights of Khorassan marched,
garbed in Arabic costumes.
The Supreme Temple of Rathbone
Sisters discussed the question of an in
surance feature and votes sufficient to
adopt the resolution on creating it have
been received.
WILL BE AWARDED
A GLITTERING TROPHY
ESSAYS BY LEAGUE OF THE
CBOSS CADETS,
Archbishop Riordai Designs a Medal
for the Best Composition on tne
Subject of Temperance.
The successful essayist In the contest
for the Archbishop Rlordan golden medal
at the annual grand rally of the League
of the Cross Cadets which will be held
at Metropolitan Hall next Sunday after
noon will be presented with a magnifi
cent diamond trophy. The medal differs
in style from any designed in the past
as a trophy to the most successful writer
on the subject of temperance, the foun
dation upon which the League of the
Cross was organized.
In the past the possessor of the medal
was aware of its purpose simply by the
inscription upon it. The winner of the
Design of the New League of
the Cross Trophy.
contest Sunday will carry with the trophy
awarded a miniature likeness of th.-
Archblshop painted upon ivory which
hangs pendent from two enameled and
decorative flags, one of the Stars and
Stripes and the other the white flag of
temperance.
The medal iÂ« made entirely of gold,
worked into various and appropriate ef
fects. The wreath surrounding the Arch
bishop's portrait is of green. The wreath
Is Joined at the bottom with a bright gold
bow knot which stands forth in pleasing
prominence against its green background.
The flags which support the lower or
pendent portion of the medal are of the
most expert workmanship, being arranged
in folds, the perfect execution of which
resembles the natural folds flags assume
when crossed. At the intersection
of the flags, and surmounting thÂ«- entire
medal is a circular piece of polished gold,
in the lower circle of which is inserted a
pure whito diamond, while the upper hall'
Is devoted to a red cross, the badge of the
organization, being outlined with a Latin
inscription in heavy black letters.
In anticipation of carrying off the glit
tering token the five competitors who won
the honor of competing for it in the sev
eral district rallies held during the past
year are striving and exerting every ef
fort to make their essays the best, that
they may receive the decision which will
entitle them to the possession of the tro
phy.

INVADING FORCES
AT WADY NAMED
Anglo-Egyptian Expedi
tion Ready to Strike.
WILL MARCH ON OMDURMAN
THEN COMES THE BATTLE WITH
THE KHALIFA.
So Far General Sir Herbert Kitchener
Has Operated Brilliantly and
Success Is Now
Predicted.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
WADY HAMED, Aug. 24.â The whole
Anglo-Egyptian expedition has arrived
here and the advance is now commen
cing to- El Hajid, where final concen
tration will be made for the march on
Omdurman, forty miles distant, which
will occupy two days, if the Khalifa
decides to await attack at Omdurman,
as now appears likely.
Refugees are arriving daily from Om
durman. They all agree that the Kha
lifa means to fight. They report that
he has 70,000 men. The attack is ex
pected to occur about September 4. Our
camp of 20,000 men, extending thrÂ« c
miles, with the white gunboats and an
innumerable fleet of native boats, pre
sents a picturesque sight to--night.
General Sir Herbert Kitchener's
achievement so far is a brilliant opera
tion. Some weeks ago he announced his
intention to concentrate his forces at
Wady Hamed on August 22. He ar
rived there to-day.
"Wady Hamed is a village forty miles
above Metammeh, and between fifty
and sixty miles from Omdurman. It is
at the foot of the long sixth cataract,
the head of which is at Shabaluka.
Considering the difficulties of moving
24,000 men and impediments over the
desert and along the Nile uftder a blaz
ing sun, the achievement is proof of the
splendid organization of the expedition
and augurs well for the final overthrow
of the Khalifa's power.
Major General Archibald Hunter
commands the Egyptian forces, which
comprise two-thirds of the expedition,
and which critics agree now form capi
tal fighting material. Major General
William Forbes Dacre commands the
British forces.
POISONS AND
CANDY-MAKING
Confectioners and Chemists Disa
gree on the Vital Points.
The vital point of interest, around which
the famous Dunning murder case re
volves, is the box of poisoned candy,
packed by careful fingers, and sent across
the continent, to carry death into the
homes of its unsuspecting victims.
A plan, diabolical in nature, careless in
detail, but fatally sure of success, on ac
count of the well-known love for candy of
the world feminine, was doubtless, carried
out by some fiend in woman's form. As
far as the public knows, this candy was
made in San Francisco. Fart of it was,
no doubt, manufactured by Home regular
confectioner, the rest by the .hand of an
hysterical, jealous woman, thinking only
of revenge upon the innocent cause of her
own unhuppiness.
The possibility of taking from the box
half of the confectioner's bon bons, sub
stituting home-made candies, and con
cealing therein, a poison not only deadly,
but tasteless, is interesting not alone to
the public, but candy manufacturers and
chemists as well.
Withing the past month, the four walls
of some room witnessed the manufacture
of dainty chocolates, tempting in appear
ance, yet tilled with death. But there was
presumably, no one to note the odor of
the boiling candy, and no one to tell
whether it was made In the early hours
of the morning, or at a time when the in
mates were all away and the murderess
alone.
"I should not think it possible," said
Mr. Gruenhagen, the confectioner, "for
a person to boil candy without the rest of
the people in the house knowing it. The
odor is very penetrating, especially that
of tames or caramels. It is not necessary
to use coloring matter to darken caramels,
and we use nothing but the sugar and
pure chocolate.
"Well, I've never tried It, but presume
poison could be introduced without it be
ing detected. Say something in the liquid
line. I don't know what effect arsenic
would have upon the candy or whether it
would mix with the sugar, but unless the
poison was very bitter the tuste of the
chocolate might cover it Up."
F. C. Warren, for ten years a manufac
turer of candies, is of the opinion that tne
ordinary inmate of a lodging-house "who
does light housekeeping could by opening
a window to let tne steam Escape or by
cooklng other things at the same time,
boil candy without the odor being de
tected."
"Why, of course," said he, "poison
could be put in without changing the ap
pearance of the candy, and 1 doubt if It
could be tasted, for the bitter of the
chocolate would obviate that. You can't
tell from the looks of candy what's in It.
It all depends upon the honesty of the
person who makes it. Any malicious per
son could put a lot of poison in this hot
candy and I would not know it by the ap
pearancfl of it."
George Haas, the well-known confec
tioner, said:
"Why, yes, I think candy could be made
In a room without any noticeable odor
fienetrating beyond the closed door. That
s. unless it waa molasses candy. When
we make taffy the whole town knows it,
but, then. New Orleans molasses always
does make itself known. So far as boiling
caramels or chocolate creams Is con
cerned, I do not think the odor would at
tract any one outside the room where
they were being made."
"Half a dozen different poisons could
be used," said Will Mack, the Market
street druggist, "but I am of the opinion
that it was strychnine; or It is possible
liquid morphine may have been poured
into the boiling candy. It could not have
been cyanide of potassium, for that would
have left not only an unpleasant taste
but an odor like bitter almonds, and would
have proved fatal more quickly. Pure
chocolate has a peculiarly bitter, acrid
taste, and that would cover the taste of
most poisons. At any rate, one swallows
candy so quickly that the poison would
have to be very pronounced to cause any
unpleasant Impression upon the palate.
It's a case of plugged watermelon,"
remarked Frank Clough. the well-known
chemist. "You se<\ sho took a very sharp
knife, cut out a little plug of the candy,
put in the arsenic, heated the candy a
trifle, smoothed it over, and there you are.
Arsenic? Of course it was arsenic, for it
would be the least liable to be detected,
and too, the length of time before it
proved fatal sounds like arsenical poison
ing. No, strychnine was not used. That
would have caused convulsions and froth
ing at the moutn. with almost Immediate
dissolution."
TJTAH STOCKMAN KILLED.
Was Settling a Quarrel Over the
Ownership of Property.
THOMPSON SPRINGS, Utah. Aug.
25. B. F. Graves, a prominent stockman
of this county, was shot and instantly
killed yesterday by Del Weant. The men
had some difference regarding the owner
ship of property. They met yesterday at
Bnrgard s ranch, and after a few words
Winchesters were brought into use, and
Graves was shot and instantly killed.

TRAGIC SUICIDE OF
A WIFE MURDERER
Ernest Joseph Passel
Kills Himself.
DIES IN A PARIS CAFE
HAD POSED AS THE MARQUIS
DE GONDEVILLE.
Jb ailed to Collect Heavy Insurance
Upon the Life of His Wife,
Whom He Threw
Over a Cliff.
. Special Dispatch to The Call.
PARIS, Aug. 25.â Ernest Joseph Possel,
the German who had been trying to col
lect a claim of Â£ll),000 upon a life insur
ance policy issued to his late wife, an
Englishwoman, by the Urbane Insurance
Company, which had resisted payment on
the ground that he had thrown her off a
cliff near Florence, Italy, committed sui
cide last night at the.Durand restaurant,
in the Place de la Madeleine.
Possell, who called himself the Marques
de Gondeville, and pretended to be a
French subject, was under heavy bail,
and the authorities had ordered the ex
humation of the body of his wife.
The maiden name of Mrs. Possel was
Eleanor a'Beckett. and she was about 21
years old. The couple had been staying
at Florence, and one day Possel returned
home alone from a drive, saying that his
wife had fallen off a cliff on the edge of
which she was resting while ho was
mending his harness. They were married
in England last March, and Possel in
sured her life almost immediately. It de
veloped after her body was found be
neath the cliff that Possel had been seen
there, several days before, throwing down
large stones and watching their descent.
Possel took a private room in the res
taurant and asked for writing materials
and breakfast. Shortly after shots were
heard. The attendants rushing in. found
him lying on the floor and dvlng. He had
shot himself in the head. Feeblj" waving
his hand toward two, letters that were
lying on the table, he asked to have them
delivered. They wore addressed to his
mother and to the Chief of Police of the
district. The letter to the police func
tionary revealed his Identity. In it Pos
sell declared that he had killed himself
because of the accusations against him
since the death of his wife.
Possel came to Paris after his release in
Italy to obtain the insurance money on
his wife's policy, but the Insurance com
pany refused to pay him until the cause
of his wife's death should have been es
tablished.
Suicide in the Park.
The body of an unknown man was found
In the Park, near Stow Lake, yesterday
morning. The deceased was about 60
years of age, of medium height with scant
pray hair and grayish mustache, dressed
in a well-worn sack suit, and was ap
parently a laboring man. He had shot
himself through the temple and the re
volver was still tightly clutched in his
right hand. There was nothing- about his
body by which it mlpht be identified.

Secretary William R. Day of Ohio.
Senator Cushman K. Davis oF Minnesota.
Senator William P. Frye oF Maine.
Justice Edward Douglas White oF Louisiana.
Whitelaw Reid oF New York.
NEW YORK, Aug. 25.â The Washing
ton correspondent of the Herald tele
graphs: These are the commissioners
definitely decided upon by the Presi
.. dent to negotiate the treaty of peace
between the United States and Spain.
The decision to appoint Justice White
... was reached yesterday, and his accept
ance was received by wire this after
noon. The formal tender of a commls
sionership to Mr. Reid was made this
morning.
The American Peace Commission ap
parently Is not going to Paris to de
mand the entire group of the Philip
â¢ pines, but to contend only for the occu
pation of Luzon Island. This was
strongly developed at a conference at I
the White House to-day between the |
.President, Secretary Day and Senators
Davis and Frye, a majority of the
. members of the commission. During the '
conference to-day the whole Spanish I
' question was fully discussed, and the :
policy of the administration was dis- !
tinctly outlined.

DEWEY'S SQUADRON
TO BE REINFORCED
Crack Warships Destined
for Manila.
OREGON WILL BE IN THE LEAD
OTHER VESSELS THAT ARE TO
â  BE SENT TO FAR EAST.
In- the Event of Complications the
Vnitea States Is Not to Be
Handicapped in Its Fight
ing Force.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
NEW YORK, Aug. 25.â A Herald spe-
c ial from Washington says: Admiral
Deweys squadron at Manila is to be
reinforced. At least three or four of
the crack vessels of the .North Atlantic
squadron will be sent to Manila in the
near future. They will go by way of
the Suez canal and will reach Asiatic
-,\ aters in the early fall. The detail for
this new eastern squadron has not yet
been completed, but It is known that
â¢ the battle-ship Oregon, the fast cruis
ers New York and Brooklyn and prob
ably the battle-ship Indiana are to be
overhauled and put in shape for the
. trip.
It is semi-officially admitted to-day
that the Oregon is sure to go, and that
it is very probable the other three ves
sels mentioned will accompany it. The
: sible reason for sending four big
fighting vessels to the far east is that
Admiral Dewey'a ships, having been in
commission so long cannot be properly
: docked and cleaned for months. As
Manila is to bo a theater in which in
ternational questions of grave import
; are to be settled within the coming six
. months the administration believes the
presence there eof a fleet of American
fighting craft is an absolute necessity.
The State Department will not admit
-the situation in Manila is anything but
tranquil now, yet it does not attempt
to. conceal the fact that should trouble
arise with Germany or any other na
tion over the disposition of the Philip
pines Admiral Dewey would be handi
capped and the nation put at a decided
disadvantage should his fleet be not
materially strengthened. From an au
thoritative source it is asserted at
least four more of the vessels of the
fighting line will be churning the path
way across the seas to Manila within
the next six weeks, !Lnd that the power
ful Oregon will be leading the proces
sion. ______ââ
SPREAD OF THE REVOLT
IN SOUTHERN CHINA
American Missionaries and Native
Converts on Hainan Island Jflee
From the Rebels.
HONGKONG, Aug. 25.â A serious Insur
â  roction has broken out at Nadoa, in the
interior of Hainan Island. American
missionaries and native converts have
Bought safety In flight. On August 14
the rebels fought the soldiers, who used
the American Presbyterian property as
defenses and repelled tho attack. Nadoa
la situated ninety miles from Hnisow, one
of the richest valleys on the island.
T'nless the revolt is speedily suppressed
the insurgents probably will be reinforced
by refugees from Kwungsi; where the of
ficial* are coping with the recent rebel
lion. Should it not be suppressed there
is an opportunity for French interfer
ence. The French are using vigorous
measures to protect the native Christians
near Kruang-Chow-Wan and are anxious
to extend their Influence in Southern
China.

Battle-Ground Selected for the Cor

BUFFALO, Aug. 26.â 1t has been defi

large majority of the American people
are Inclined to favor the taking of the
Philippines by the United States, the
participants in the conference to-day
advanced what they considered sub
stantial reasons why this should not be
done. In order to obtain absolute con
trol over the entire group of islands the

WOULD DISBAND
GARCIA'S ARMY
Santiago Merchants Say
Business Is Menaced.
CUBANS PREVENT TRADE
AT PRESENT PLANTATIONS CAN
NOT BE WORKED.
Commercial Operations of the Prov
ince Paralyzed Because No
Produce Is Coming In
From the Country.
Special Cable to The Call and the New York
Herald. Copyrighted. UM, by James Gor
don Bennett.
SANTIAGO DE CUBA, Aug. 25.â 1
talked to-day with many merchants of
this city and every one of them was
anxious that the Cuban army should
be disbanded at once. It Is a standing
menace to business, they say. They all
are anxious to resume, but found it
impossible to do so because property is
threatened by the ill-disciplined levies
of Garcia's command.
Cultivators refuse to return to plan
tations, where they will be subject to
contributions for the support of the
Cuban soldiers, who are still in the
field. No produce therefore will come
to market here and ships that bring
cargoes cannot take anything back. If
the province Is to return to a self-sup
porting basis and trade begin again the
Cuban army. It Is generally admitted,
must be disbanded.
Robert Mason, British Consul here,
who is locai manager for Brooks & Co.,
told me that his business is absolutely
at a standstill.
"No produce is coming from the
country,'' he said, "because the condi
tion of things there Is unsettled and
merchants are afraid to engage in op
erations until the political situation is
better defined. Owing to the lack of
labor, lighters and wharfage facilities
we are completely blocked and our
business Interests are suffering. We
cannot order cargoes. There is no
money In the province. If trade is to
be resumed some definite step must be
taken immediately by the United
States."
I have Just returned from a visit to
San Luis, where I made inquiries aa
to the truth of reports of outrages com
mitted by men of the Eighth Illinois
that had come to us here. The Eighth
is stationed in San Lifis. These reports
were brought in by Cubans. I found
they had been much exaggerated.

Men Who Will Negotiate the Treaty With Spain.
AGUINALDO READY
TO CO-OPERATE WITH
THE AMERICANS
Complains of a Lack of Honesty and Ability
Among Rebel Leaders and Is
Anxious to Disband.
BY SOL N. SHERIDAN.
MANILA, Aug. 25. â I have just had an interview with
General Aguinaldo in Bacoor. The insurgent leader de
clares he is anxious to support the authority of the United
States in these islands and that he has persuaded other
rebel leaders to accept his views. It is his desire that the
insurgent army be disbanded and return to the provinces.
He complains of lack of honesty and military talent in the
rebel leaders and says he has not an army but only an un
ruly rabble. The general seemed dispirited. He adds that
he trusts the United States will form a free and liberal gov
ernment, and says the Americans can count on his co-op
eration.
The rebellion is spreading in the south. Sorsogon has
fallen into the hands of the rebels, five Spaniards being
killed in the assault.

TIMOTHY HEALY TALKS
ABOUT HOME RULE
One Stand the United States Might
Take That Would Cause Bless
ings in Ireland.
DUBLIN. Aug. 25.â Timothy Healy,
member of Parliament for North Louth,
addressing a meeting at Dublin this
evening, said he regarded the new local
government act as the vestibule of home
rule. There were two ways, he said, of
winning the latterâ either by turning the
fleets and armies against the British
Government, or by enlisting the sympa
thies of British statesmen. He favored
the latter policy, and therefore consid
ered the refusal of the Dublin corpora
tion to join in a national memorial to
Mr. Gladstone as one of the gravest
blunders ever committed by any public
body.
In conclusion, ho said: "If America
should insist that Ireland were equally
entitled to self-government with Cuba
and should refuse any friendship or alli
ance with England until that was
granted. Incalculable blessings would re
sult."

Quay and what he termed the machine
politics of the State.
A vigorous campaign â will at once be>
started and meetings will be held in
every county in the State.

IDENTIFYING RAILWAY CAR
RIAGES.
Who that has traveled by railway has
not experienced a difficulty In finding ns
seat again once he has got out at an In
termediate station? You get out to get a
glass of beer or a hurried snack at the
refreshment buffet, and when you get
back to the platform you wander along
the train, looking into every compartment
to identify the luggage you have left !n
order to keep your seat. The Western of
France Railway has come to the rescue
of its passengers in this respect. On Us
express train in the morning from Paris
to St. Malo and Parame it has put car
riages with pictures upon them. One has
an elephant, another a lyre, another a
snake, etc. There are anchors, hunts
man's horns, balloons, tricolored flags,
bunches of grapes, swallow?, a pair of
scales, a star, an angler with his fishing
rod. It is thought that one of these de
signs will be more readily remembered
than a number, and. according to ail ac
counts, passengers are very pleased with
the innovation. Before long the Western
of France directors hope to have picture
trains on all their lines.â London News.

GERMANY WOULD
PARTITION THE
SAMOAN ISLANDS
Inspires a Feeler for the Purpose
of Sounding* America and
England.
This Country Will Not Listen to the Proposal.
Correspondence That May Lead to
Serious Friction-
NEW YORK. Aug. 25.â The Herald's Washington correspondent
sends the following: Trouble is brewing over the Samoan Islands,
which under the treaty of 1890 are governed under a joint protect
orate by the United States, Great Britain and Germany. The recent
proposition advanced by the Marine Politlsche Correspondenz that the
islands should be divided, England t.-".ing Suval, Germany Upolu and
America Tutuila, it is believed here, was inspired by the German
Government as a feeler for the purpose of finding out how the .United
States and Great Britain would receive the proposition. This propo
sition would give Germany the best of the islands and Great Britain
the worst.
As far as the United States is concerned the proposition to par
tition the group will not be considered for an instant. Correspond
ence is now in progress between the United States and Germany that
may lead to serious friction. It is thought probable that there will
have to be eventually a new commission to frame a new treaty, and
if so it is likely Germany will then propose to partition the islands.
The United States will not consent to this. Great Britain has always
taken a rather passive interest in Samoan affairs, but there is no
doubt that her influence will in the future, as in the past, be thrown
on the side of the United States.
SIXTEEN LOST IN
COLLISION AT SEA
French Fishingr Vessel
Sunk by the Norge.
CUT IN TWO BY THE LINER
CAPTAIN AND EIGHT SEAMEN
PICKED UP.
Rescue of the Others Was Impossi
ble â Disaster Happens in a
Fog Off the Grand
"Pan kg
Special Dispatch to The Call.
NEW TORK, Aug. 25.â The Thing
valla line steamer Norge, which arrived
to-day, reports that she sank the
French fishing schooner La Coquette of
Bayonne, France, on Saturday last on
the Grand Banks. The captain and
eight seamen were saved, but sixteen
men went down with the vessel.
The Norge sailed from Stettin August
3. The weather was generally fine to
the banks of Newfoundland, when it
became foggy with patches of clear
weather. Saturday, August 20, between
3 and 4 o'clock in the afternoon, the
weather was foggy, but not so thick
that the vessel's speed was reduced.
Captain Knudson said he could see
about three cable lengths ahead. The
wind was fairly brisk from the west
southwest, when a vessel suddenly
loomed from the north with sails full
and stood directly across the bow of the
steamer. The bells were rung to stop
and back at full speed, but too late to
check the steamer's headway. The
stranger, a fishing schooner, fell across
the bow and with a crash was forced
over and sunk. Three men sprang
aboard the Norge.
The passengers of the Norge, most of
whom were about the decks, rushed
about in alarm at the shock, but were
soon quieted when they learned thai
the steamer was uninjured. A boat
was lowered and six men and a dog
were picked up. The vessel sank al
most immediately, and in sinking car
ried with her sixteen of her crew.
Captain Knudson said that, as a
steam vessel, the Norge was obliged to
keep out of the way of all sailing craft,
but this was a time when the sailing
vessel could do more to help herself
than a steamer.
â  The latter was going ahead at full
speed, but there was not time, in the
judgment of the captain, to reduce the
speed. The fisherman was an active
vessel, had a good breeze and was un
der good steerage way, but made no at
tempt to avoid the collision, keeping on
: with sails full until squarely under the
bow. The large hull of the Norge,
Captain Knudson said, should have
been visible for a long -distance.
At the office of the French Consul the
following names of the lost were given:
Lemant, Gannen, Allain, Lepron, Mon
slnet, Graffet, Curnitem, Halmgont,
Lescot, Iforquet, Gerard, Guernara, De
crotx, Minguri, Buurel and Bonper. All
were fishermen.
'"lit- report of Captain Berre of La
Coquett<* to the French Consul states
that when the collision occurred there
! was a heavy fog prevailing. The men
were all on deck fishing. Suddenly the
Norge appeared coming toward them,
head on. for their side. It was impos
sible to do anything to get out of the
way. The Norge, which was going at
, a speed of from eight to ten knots an
â  hour, struck La Coquette in the side.
! Her af termast fell toward the wheel.
j There were sixteen men in that part of
I the vessel and the rigging, sails and
, mast pinned them down, so that it was
inevitable death for them as the vessel
began to sink. The wheel was smashed
by the fallen mast' and control of La
Coquette was lost.
The Norge cut La Coquette clean in
i half. The steamer backed ;as quickly
as possible and; her crew lowered boats
and v went to the rescue of ~ the -â¢ men in
j the water. It was impossible to save
the sixteen men 1 who perished. La Co
j quette sank : at ; once. 7 â

aimless. My telegram of July 27 was in

After a long series of sleepless nights

pipes, one after the -other. I felt no un
easiness in leaving him. He was, in
deed, so well that I invited him to take
champagne after having forbidden him

really drink some sec again?' cried the

"i remember not lontr since listening

I could but interfere in this disgusting

inger. my trumpet will >ound no longer
It no longer holds wind.'

ous times.' he would say.

ALL DID NOT TAKE
OATH OF ALLEGIANCE
Some Members of the Hawaiian Reg
iment Who Refused to Desert
Their Native Flag.
BY ALICE RIX.
Special Correspondence of The Call.
HONOLULU, Aug. 18â After the
'â  raising of the American Flag over the
I Executive Building at Honolulu, the
Hawaiian Regiment, carrying the na
tional flag of Hawaii furled, was
marched over to the Barracks, asked to
THE SAN FRAS CISCO CALL,, FKIDAY, AUGL^ST 26, IS9B.
PRESENTATION OF THE STARS AND STRIPES TO THE NATIVE HAWAIIAN
REGIMENT.
; take the oath of allegiance to the
United States of America and pre
sented with the old American flag
j which was used on the United States
! cruiser Boston.
The scene was touching in the ex
treme. The men of the Hawaiian
I Regiment wept freely and several of
j them, eight or nine out of a single com-
I pany, refused positively to take
I the * oath. While their comrades
| uncovered and with uplifted hand
! received and acknowledged the
i oath of allegiance to an alien land,
I they stood sullenly with their caps
1 drawn over their eyes and their guns
< clasped in their hands, blind and d ftf
; alike to the urging of their officers and
j the example of their brothers in
i arms.
Colonel Fisher presented the Ameri
can Flag with an appropriate speech.
The old flag from the Boston has
seen better days. Its red and blur- are
faded, its white is dingy and its stars
are dim. This, which would make it
all the dearer to Americans, was re
ceived as something of a slight by the
sensitive and so recently wounded Ha
walians. Many of the men of the
Regiment regarded it with ill-concealed
contempt, and their weeping friends
and sweethearts in the crowd gathered
about the grounds, smiled derisively as
the faded flag went up when the giy
Hawaiian colors had Just been low
ered.
One hearty American cheer saved the
moment to both Hawaiians and Amer
icans as Old Glory went marching by.

AS A PROLOGUE TO
TROUBLE IN EUROPE
How the Madrid Liberal Regards the
War Between the United States
and Spain.
MADRID, Aug. 25.â The Liberal In a
leading article says: "England wishes
the annexation of the Philippines by
the United States in order to use them
as a base of operations against France
and Russia." It also says the war be
tween Spain and the United States is a
prologue to European complications in
which Spain will be involved.
The Government has authorized Gen
eral Maclas to dissolve the militia in
Porto Rico.

BEFORE NOVEMBER
Spaniards to Be Given Considerable
Time in Withdrawing From
the Island of Cuba.
NEW YORK, Aug. 25.â The Herald's
Washington correspondent telegraphs:
I understand that the Instructions to
the Cuban commissioners do not con
template the evacuation of Cuba before
November 1, though no specific date is
mentioned. A Cabinet officer told me
to-night that the American flag will
probably not be floating o-ver Havana
before that date. The American army
of occupation, he said, would not start
for Cuba before the latter part of Oc
tober.

popular member of the senior class, was
unanimously elected manager of the new
football team. It Is the intention to or
ganize a baseball nine in the near future.

HAD A PLEASANT
TRIP TO HONOLULU
Culiom and Hitt Talk of
the Voyage.
MARIPOSA'S ACCOMMODATION
ALL THAT COTTLD BE EXPECT
ED AT PRESENT.
Commissioners Declare That Every
thing Possible Was Done Dur
ing the Voyage for Their
Comfort and Pleasure.
I
Special Correspondence of The Call.
BY ALICE RIX
HONOLULU, Aug. 18.â The Commis
sioners arrived in Honolulu by the
Oceanic steamship Mariposa yesterday
morning at 5:30 o'clock.
As soon as the San Francisco papers
were distributed, I called personally on
Senator Cullom, who is at the Hawai
ian Hotel, and showing him a copy of
a San Francisco morning paper of the
11th inst., which printed certain state
ments purporting to come from him
and reflecting seriously on the accom-
modations and management of the
Oceanic Steamship Company's line of
steamers and the business character
of one of its proprietors.
Senator Cullom had not seen the is
sue of the paper in question and ex
pressed great surprise when he learned
that his name had been used and in a
manner by no means flattering to his
dignity or his position as United States
Commissioner.
He gave me full authority to deny
flatly and in whatever terms I might
ccc fit the statements attributed to
"What I did say and what I did
feel," he said, "was Just this: "We were
going to the islands on business of im
portance to the Government of the
United States and, as a commission, we
were disappointed to find that better
accommodation could not be given us
for the ocean trip to the islands, to
which we had looked forward as the
pleasantest stage of our journey. We
found the Mariposa crowded with pas
sengers and freight â which," and Sena
tor Cullom bowed politely, "is a tribute
to the popularity of the line. "We had
ladies in our party and we regretted
they could not have had roomier quar
ters. We did not know â I did not ask
at leastâ until I was directly Informed
in San Francisco of the demands made
on the Oceanic line by reason of send
ing American troops on to Manila, and,
in my first annoyance at finding it
must be cramped quarters or no quar
ters until another sailing day, I did not
consider the matter from any stand
point but that of our personal incon
venience. I understand here in Ho
nolulu that the demand for food sup
plies and other freight has been far in
excess of the means of supply. Now
you may quote me as saying further
that our trip was one of the most en
joyable I have ever taken In any dl- |
rectlon or under any circumstances.
Everything was done for our comfort
and pleasure and I shall be pleased If
you will make this statement In my ;
name. I have made a vow, founded â¢
on sad experiences of the past, never
to give a signed statement to any
newspaper, but you may use my name
in this matter as freely as you please.
I leave It, with perfect security, In your
hands." *
Congressman Hltt. speaking In rela
tion to statements attributed to him
regarding the "using as a freight boat"
of the steamship carrying the United
States Commission, said: "Of course
we were all slightly annoyed at the sit
uation, but no blame ran be attached
to the Oceanic Steamship Company.
Through an act of war their service
has been severely crippled. Their
steamers are being used to carry Old
Glory to the farthermost ends of the
Pacific, and It would be most unpa
triotic on our part to censure the men
to whose enterprise the quick trans
portation of American troops to Ma
nila has been due. We know that the
people of Honolulu have suffered con
siderable inconvenience by being de
prived of necessary food supplies, and
I can say for our party that the slight
annoyance we were put to counts as
nothing as against relieving the situa
tion in these islands which have been
of bo much benefit to our Government."
FAMOUS "FAMILY
COMPACr IS BROKEN
Political Machine of Hawaii Will Be-
come Demoralised Under the
New Turn of Affairs.
HONOLULU. Augr. 18.â The fa
mous "family compact," the politi
cal machine which not only direct
ed hut dictated the government of af
fairs of the so-called republic of Ha
waii, is broken.
In fact, it has been literallr smashed
to pieces, and the pieces have been
gathering themselves together and
forming into little minor machines,
each one of which is buoyed with
the hope of running things after
the commission has made its re
port and Congress has decided
what form of government is best
adapted to the peculiar conditions of
the islands.
The missionaries who conducted a re
public by a few people and for a few
people are discovering that being a
part and parcel of the United StateÃ is
not all that it was cracked up to be.
They are asking one another, "Where
are we at?" and the guesses are not
always reassuring.
True, the Dole Government is still
managing the affairs of Hawaii under a
provision of the Newlands resolution,
but ex-Mlnister Sewall is to all intents
and purposes temporary Governor of
the islands, and the change is not at
all to the liking of the men who for
rive years have perpetuated themselves
in offlce and grown fat and independ
ent at the public crib.
The action of the American Union
party, which is undoubtedly the ruling
political power in the islands, in openly
advocating Sewall for Governor has
caused consternation in tha ranks of
Dole and his Cabinet. While apparent
ly in sympathy with the Dole party,
they have been secretly working for the
ex-Minister, and confidently expected
that President McKinley's proclama
tion would name Sewall for Governor,
with full power to select a new Cabinet.
The patronage to be distributed by this
arrangement was what the American
Union party banked on. and now that
the expected change has not taken
place they are in a measure left en
tirely out in the cold.
The proclamation sent here by the
President was very vague, to say the
least, and the American Union party
dispatched by the last mail a strong
letter to Washington asking for ex
plicit instructions, and thoroughly de
fining their position. The terms of Dis
trict Judges and other minor officers
come to an end on September 1, and the
politicians want a finger in the pie
when the new appointees are named.
Considerable patronage is attached to
these offices, and the Filters, the cous
ins and the aunts of the "family com
pact" are liable to find themselves out
of a job when the instructions reach
here.
That Dole and his Cabinet are con
cerned about this new development of
things politic is evident from the un
natural desire of several of the party to
'retire to private life." It was not sup
posed that this element was anywhere
to be found in their compositions.
Attorney General Smith has already
sent in his resignation, and several oth
ers of the close corporation are leaning
strongly in the same direction.
A great and freezing coldness has
come between President Dole and Min
ister Sewall even in this land of glori
ous sunshine. Mr*. Dole is said to
j have snubbed Mrs. Sewall at the now
! famous "democratic" ball given In
j honor of the fin "-raising, and this seri
ous political rupture has set the gos
sipy tongues of all Honolulu to wag
ging furiously.
So the new political pot of Hawaii
boils merrily, and until definite instruc
tions arrive'from Washington every one
from Sewall down to the Janitor of the
executive building will still keep ask-
I ing, "Where do I get off?"

NOW FRAMING A
GOVERNMENT FOR
HAWAIIAN ISLANDS
Members of the Commission Sent
From the United States
Reach Honolulu.
Upon Arrival They Exchange Courtesies With
Dole and Cabinet and Then Com
mence Their Labors.
HONOLULU, Aug. IS.â The steam
ship Mariposa and the steam schooner
Lakme arrived almost simultaneously
about half-past 5 o'clock yesterday
morning. The troops on the Lakme
were in excellent health, and had no
cases of sickness to report. They were
landed on the arrival of Colonel Barber
and in due course marched down to the
temporary encampment grounds at
Kapiolani Park.
Senators Cullom and Morgan and
Representative Hitt of the Hawaiian
Congressional Committee and their
contingent also arrived in perfect
health. They were greeted on board
by ex-Hawaiian Ministers to Washing
ton. Hon. F. M. Hatch and Hon. W. R.
Castle, and a few others, including
Senator Morgan's previous and pres
ent host, A. M. Ballou, the compiler of
our new code system.
"Over Seas," near Nuuana Valley,
will be Senator Morgan's domicile,
while Senator Cullom will reside at
suite 17 of the Royal Hawaiian Hotel,
made notable as the former dwelling of
ex-Secretary John W. Foster and many
other distinguished personages. Rep
resentative Hitt occupies the rebuilt
Snow cottage, adjacent to the hotel,
also distinguished as the former resi
dence of "Paramount" Blount, United
States Ministers Merrill and Stevens,
Senator Pettigrew and others.
The Commissioners exchanged cour
tesies yesterday afternoon with the
President and Cabinet, Hon. H. M. Se
v.all. Admiral Miller and the repre
sentatives of the press, and other dis
tinguished citizens. This morning they
visited their fellow Commissioner, As
sociate Justice of the Supreme Court
Walter F. Frear, who is invalided at
his residence, and there he took the
oath of office. This afternoon they will
meet to personally organize, and as
soon as possible and before setting to
work will proceed on a tour of inspec
tion of the island of Hawaii and prob
ably of all the principal islands of the
Hawaiian archipelago. Next week the
native Hawaiian .patriotic associations
will hold a formal meeting to appoint
a committee of conference with the
Commissioners.
Even in the few hours they have been
here Messrs. Cullom and Hitt have
created a most favorable impression
upon all who have had the pleasure of
meeting them; Senator Morgan, of
course, is well remembered by his
friends, and especially so by his oppo
nents, who are very uncharitably and
unforgivingly reminiscent.
It is practically useless to give you
interviews with them, for they have
said nothing of any importance or upon
which anything of the nature of infor
mation or of a line of action could be
based, except that they desire accurate
information of the conditions of the
country and the people before they
proceed to deliberative action. They
are rather seekers than imparters of
news. Acting in an advisory and not a
legislative capacity they are willing, if
consulted, to tender their advice to the
temporary Government on local mat
ters, especially in connection with the
loan acts passed by the last Legislature
to aid in internal improvements and
such like matters M would not in
trench upon the privileges and func
tions of Congress. Their future place
of deliberations will probably be the
legislative hall of the House of Repre
sentatives, the ancient throne room, as
there they will be in close connection
with the officials of the Government
and the archives. The sessions will be
partially of an executive nature, but
not strictly so, as they will adopt the
Blount precedent of sending for per
sons and papers and of listening to
suggestions. They decline most direct
ly to indicate the form of government
they may feel inclined to favor, and no
one can honestly blame them for their
reticence on a matter that they are
scarcely qualified to discuss upon so
brief an acquaintance. It is, however,
the feeling of those best qualified to
judge that If their investigations, as
undoubtedly they will be. are consci
entiously and thoroughly pursued, a
very liberal form of government will be
advised. One of the main contentions
will be that this has been a self-gov

HORACE WRIGHT.
OFFICERS ELECTED
BY THE WOODMEN
CONSTITUTIONAL AMENDMENTS
BEING CONSIDERED.
A Banquet to the Head Camp Officer*
and Delegates at the California
Hotel Last Night.
The third session of the Head Camp of
the Pacific Jurisdiction of the Woodmen
of the World held yesterday was devoted
wholly to the nomination and election of
Head Camp officers.
The choica was as follows: Head
consul, F. A- Falkenburg of Denver,
(re-elected); adviser, Richard Fahy of
San Francisco; clerk. General J. W.
Browning: of Denver, Col. (re-elected);
banker, C. V. Cooper of Portland. Or.;
managers. A. D. Stillman, Helena, Mont.;
J. C. Latshaw, Pueblo, Col., and I. I.
Boak of Oakland; escort, E. 8. Ewing of
Fresno; watchman, N. O. Baldwin of
Pomeroy. Wash.; sentry, W. M. Elliott of
Salt Lake.
The camp then adjourned until this
morning when the amendments offered
will be considered.
At the session of delegates held in Odd
Fellows' Hall on Wednesday night there
were present a large number of mem
bers of the camps of this city, Oakland
and Alameda. The Sovereign and Head
Camp officers were cordially greeted,
after which the ritualistic work was ex
emplified by Oakland Lodge, which rave
the protection degree in the amplified
form, which is intensely dramatic and
interesting. The opening work was by
Golden Gate Camp and the closing work
by Redwood Camp. After that there were
addresses by Sovereign Commander J. C.
Root, Head Consul Faikenburg, Head
Clerk General J. W. Browning and ex-
Senator H. G. Gesford.
Yesterday the session of the Women ot
Woodcraft was taken up in the discus
sion of amendments to the constitution.
Last night the Head Camp officers and
delegates were the guests of the recep
tion committee of the local camps at a
banquet tendered them at the California
Hotel in the banquet room, which was
decorated with patriotic colors. There
were present about 150 Woodmen and a
few specially invited guests in addition to
the Columbia Quartet of Mission Camp
which rendered choice vocal selections
during the evening. The toastmaster was
Head Auditor-elect Robert Fahy, who,
after the black coffee was brought on,
called on the speakers of the evening.

Mrs. Romualdo Pacheco arrived last

The opening production will be a n^w

SANTA BARBARA
HONORS A HERD
i
Funeral of Young Stan
ley Hollister.
BANKS AND SCHOOLS CLOSED
AND BUSINESS GENEBALLY SUS
PENDED.
Civil War Veterans and Naval Re
serves Escort the Body of the
Rough Rider to Its Last
Resting Place.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
SANTA BARBARA, Aug. 25.â Never
in the history of Santa Barbara have
such high marks of honor and respect
fceen bestowed upon the remains of one
of its citizens as were shown this af
ternoon at the funeral of Stanley Hol
lister, late of Roosevelt's Rough Riders.
Flags have been flying at half-mast
since his death, and this afternoon the
banks and business houses all
closed their doors and the schools
dismissed their pupils out of respect to
his memory.
The veterans of the Civil War, both
Confederate and Federal, about a
hundred In number, marched together
from the Grand Army Hall to
Knight's undertaking parlors, where
they were jojned by the Naval Re
serves of this city and Company A of
the local sharpshooters, and from there
they led the funeral procession to Trin
ity Church. Arriving there the mili
tary companies faced in line, the vet
erans bared their heads and the other
two presented arms as the body was
borne into the church.
The church was beautifully decorated
with floral decorations and national col
ors, and the solemn and beautiful ser
vice of the Episcopal church was read.
The ceremony was not a prolonged one,
the Rev. Mr. "Wakefield of San Jose of
ficiating in the absence of Rector Ram
! sey. The body was taken to the ceme
tery for Interment, it being a private
affair, and was witnessed only by the
near relatives.
Thus passed to the grave all that was
mortal of one of the most popular of
young men. He was the youngest son
of the late W. W. Hollister, whose
name is so closely connected with early
California and early Santa Barbara
history. A graduate of Harvard in its
literary department, a student of its
law school, he was among the first to
â  lay down his books and erasp his
musket in his country's service. Thrice
wounded in his short service he re
covered to fall a victim to malarial fe
ver.
INTERESTS THE PEOPLE
OF THE PACIFIC COAST
A Ruling by the Internal Revenue
Commissioner at the Request of
California Wine Men.
WASHINGTON, Aug. 25.â The Commis
sioner of Internal Revenue modified his de
cision of July 5. I&9S, to the extent of
holding that when foreign or domestic
wines are transferred from bulk pack
ages to bottles in an immature state for
.storage in bins for a considerable length
of time for aging purposes, wine so trans
ferred to bottles will not be regarded as
having been "bottled for sale" until such
time as it arrives at a marketable con
dition, and the stamping of the bottle
may be deferred until then. As soon as
wines are in a marketable condition the
liability to the tax at once attaches and
stamps must be affixed. The affixing of
caps or labels to such bottles will be
taken as 'conclusive evidence that they
ar>- "bottled for sale," and if proper
stamps have not been attached prior to
that time the bottle must be stamped
without fail when caps and labels are
affixed. This ruling is rendered at the
urgent request of the associated vltieul
turlsts and wine merchants of California.

STANLEY HOLLISTER.
YOSEMITE PARK
FREE FROM SHEEP
Miller & Lux Greatest
Trespassers.
FEELING AGAINST THE FIRM
HAS VAST TRACTS OF LAND OF
ITS OWN.
Four Herders Arrested by the New
Commissioner â Utah. "Volunteer
Cavalry Now Guarding
the Reservation.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
WAWONA, Cal., Aug. 25.â The Yo
semite Park, for the first time since it
became a national park, has been this
summer under the supervision of civil
ians. The custom has been to have the
park guarded and patrolled by the sol
diers of the regular army. The war
with Spain, however, caused the War
Department to send the soldiers in
tended for here to the Philippines, in
consequence of which Yosemlte Park
was without guard or patrol until June
25, when J. W. Zevely arrived to
take charge of the park. He immedi
ately gathered some of the best moun
taineers In this section and sent them
out into the park, one detail being in
charge of Special United States Land
Agent H. C. Cullom, and the other un
der Special Agent W. A. Buck.
They have done excellent work, not
withstanding the fact that the park
was being overrun with sheep at the
time, as the parties in charge of the
sheep had all the spring to drive their
flocks into the park. One hundred and
seventy-five thousand sheep have been
ejected from the park by the two de-
tails.
Special Agent Cullom returned yes
terday, having In charge four herders
whom he had under arrest for trespass
ing, their flocks being found in the
park. The sheep they had in charge
was the property of Miller & Lux and
G. W. Mordecai of Madera. Quite a
feeling exists among people here
against Miller & Lux for grazing nu
merous bands of sheep in the park, as
the firm is known to have thousands of
acres of land of its own. The herders
of these flocks have been ejected once,
and notified that if they returned they
would be arrested, but as they did not
heed the warning and were found in the
park, they were arrested and brought
here, their sheep being scattered by the
special agents.
Quite a number of other bands of
sheep have been found and have been
scattered, and other arrests have been
made..
Owing to the existence of numerous
and extensive fires In the park an extra
force of men was engaged by Super
intendent Zevely to fight them, and
they are now under control. Utah
Volunteer Cavalry which left San
Francisco on the 13th arrived here to
day. They are under command of Cap
tain Came, and are to guard the To
semite Park the balance of this season
under the direction of Superintendent
Zevely. The troops go into quarters at
Camp A. E. Wood, formerly occupied
by the regular troops.

Cuba's political future in next Sun
day's Call.

THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, FRIDAY, AUGrTTST 26, 1898.
GOSSIP OF THE
RACETRACK
Flyers Ready for the
State Fair Meeting.
HOST THAT WORKED WELL
EX-LONG DISTANCE CHAMPION
GILEAD DOING NICELY.
Local Plungers Many Thousands
Ahead at the Far East â News
of California Racing Men
and Horses.
Gallopers, trotters and pacers are re
ceiving their "preps' for the State Fair
meeting 1 at Sacramento, which begins
September 5. Oakland, Sacramento.
Fresno, Los Angeles and other "horsey"
cities will have good representatives at the
(â¢timing meeting, and the number of good
horses in training insures a rousing meet
in the capital city of California.
Burns & Waterhouse have about twenty
head in training at the Oakland course
nearly, if not all, two-year-olds. Trainer
Ben Timmons has been sending mout of
them along pretty fast.
George Bertolacd's Olive, Major Cook
and a few others are doing nicely at the
same track, and Frank Taylor has a few
in charge and doing well.
The Messrs. Boots left a large string at
the Elmwood ranch, Milpitas, and it is
expecn-d that they will have several rep
resentatives by Brutus at the Sacramento
meeting.
Nkk S. Hall Is expected to return from
Chicago with his flyers In. time to take
part in the running, and most of the Cali
fornia strings at present racing in Mon
tana will have returned by the time the
first bell rings at Agricultural Park.
From Sacranlento we received the fol
lowing anent the horses In training:
Tom Boyle's Morlnga is looking and go-
Ing well, and the same can be said of J.
Welsh's Lump and stable companion,
down from Woodland.
Goldscratch, by imp Goldfinch-Helen
Scratch, has as much speed as ever, but
It is extremely doubtful if he ever fancies
going a long Journey, for he tires at the
finish of his workouts.
Scarborough, the California Derby win
ner, and the consistent Harry Thoburn, of
Atkln & Lottridge's string (leased now,
however, to others), are shaping up ex
ceedingly well and will take some beating
when they "go to the races."
Covlngton's Carbineer seems to be con
siderably Improved over his laÂ«t spring's
form, and is working most satisfactorily.
\\ . Gardiner's sister to Candelaria has
a lot of speed and works along like a race
mare.
There is a black filly in Martin's string
that works well enough to make most
folks think she should win early at the
coming meet.
Gilead, the ex-champion long-distance
horse, which has been "on the shelf" for
a couple of years, bounds along with ap
parently more speed than ever, and if his

DEADLY POISON
IN ICE CREAM
Many Persons Made 111
by Eating It.
THREE OF THE VICTIMS DIE
AT LEAST AS MANY MORE FA
TALITIES EXPECTED.
Guests at a New York Summer Re
sort Succumb to a Delicacy
Flavored With Lemon
Extract.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
MIDDLETOWN, N. V.. Aug. 25.â Ice
cream prepared with lemon extract,
purchased from a traveling salesman a
few days ago, has caused the death of
three persons, and a score of others are
sick and more fatalities are expected.
The dead a^-e: Mrs. Herman Mlch
aelis, 613 Ninth avenue. New York;
Mrs. William Seder, Mount Vernon, N.
V. ; Robert Jones, a farmer living near
Greenfield, N. Y.
The seriously sick are: H. J. Bard
ing, New York; Herman Musdereh,
Mount Vernon; Mark Holmes, New-
York; Mrs. Snyder and daughter, New
York; Mrs. Hanson, New York; Mrs.
D. A. Oaborne, Newburg; Mrs. C. E.
Allen and two children. New York;
Mrs. Catherine Taylor, Philadelphia;
Miss Alice C. Leaton, Brooklyn; Henry
Weber, New York; Mrs. Arthur Jones
and three sons, Greenfield, N. V. ; Mr.
and Mrs. William Jones, Mountain
Dale.
These constitute nearly all the sum
mer guests at Arthur Jones' cottages
at Greenfield, which is about five miles
from Mountain Dale; also the proprie
tor's parents and family. The ice cream
was eaten at Sunday's dinner, and
about 9 o'clock that night Mrs. Seder
was attacked with nausea. Soon after
others were similarly seized, and by
midnight the two cottages were filled
with sick. Dr. J. F. Curlette of Moun
tain Dale was summoned, and he diag
nosed the cases as ptomaine poisoning.
He called in Dr. Munson of Wood
burne, and the two have worked inces
santly. Mrs. Seder died Tuesday, Mrs.
Michaelis Wednesday night, and early
this morning Robert Jones died.
Dr. Curlette believes six deaths will
occur.

Foreign-Born Chinese.
Some time ago Collector Jackson re
fused to l:in<l four Chinese children â Lee
Lan Hing, aged 5; We Wing Tack, aged
6; Soon Cow, aged 7, and Fung Tsoy,
aged 10. The parents of the first two
were born in this country, and the de
partment has overruled Collector Jack
fion, on the ground that, as the parents
w.ere born here, they are entitled to land,
even though they were born in China.
Tin- other two, however, were the chil
dren . m< rchantfl, born in China,
but living in this country, and they must
go back to China.

SLY TACTICS
ARE EMPLOYED
Want Overhead Trolley
Systems.
MARKET-ST. COMPANY AWAKE
AIMING TO OBTAIN A CHEAP
LINE DOWNTOWN.
E. P. E. Troy, the insurance Man,
Makes Some Statements Bear
ing Strongly on Thig
Point.
The statement is made by E. P. E. Troy,
the California street insurance man, that
the Market Street Railway Company is
bringing various influences to bear to ul
timately secure the privilege of stringing
overhead trolley wires along Market
street. Mr. Troy said yesterday: "In or
der to lead gradually up to this end the
company recently suggested to the Mer
chants' Association the circulation of the
printed query blank, of which mention
has been made in The Call, asking prop
erty owners and tenants on Montgomery,
Sansome and adjacent streets if they de
slred an electric double track service on
Sixth to Market and Taylor, along iay
lor to Post, on Post to Montgomery, with
a single track service, using turnouts, on
Montgomery to Jackson, along Jackson
to Sansome, and further, with double
tracks from Sansome to Market. The
question was also put, as an evident al
ternative. 'Do you desire all tracks on
your street to be taken up and no street
car service there?' While the statement
was not directly made, the inference was
plain that if the electric service was not
desired no service of any kind would be
furnished.
â¢'Now," continued Mr. Troy, "the pro
posed electric lines are all within me tire
limits of the city, and there is serious
objection to the erection of overhead
wires on these business 'streets, both on
account of the interference with the tele
phone wires, the increased danger from
lire and the other risks incident to the
stringing of highly charged wires over
the center of busy streets. Many prop
erty owners have filled out the query
blanks thoughtlessly in favor of the elec
tric overhead wires, not having consid
ered the consequences. They do not bear
in mind the fact that the granting of the
privilege asked will place another elec
tric wire across Market street and also
bring two other wires down to ft. There
are already two electric wires crossing
Market streetâ one at Third and another
at Fourth. Now how easy it will be, if
the company secures the new concessions,
to come forward after a while and ask
the city for the privilege of connecting
the various ends of wires along Market
street by running an overhead trolley
down that thoroughfare. The company
sees ahead. It is gradually encroaching
so as not to attract attention and opposi
tion.
"The company, by going at the matter
in the way that it has. cleverly manages
to give the Board of Supervisors an ex
cuse to grant the privilege for overhead
trolleys on the strength of the claim that
the property owners want them erected.
In truth, very few of the citizens whose
interests are actually involved have
signed the petition."
DIRECTORS TO ACT.
Consider a Request to Operate the
Sixth and Post Street Lines
by Electricity.
A meeting of the directors of the Mar
ket Street Railway Company was held
yesterday afternoon. General Manaeer
E. P. Vining read the letter which he had
received from the Merchants' Association
in regard to changing the Sixth street and
Post street lines to electric roads and
asking for various other transfer privi
leges. Manager Vining was authorized to
use his own judgment in regard to the
new transfer system asked for. The ex
ecutive committee of the board of direc
tors will decide as to the advisability of
operatine the two lines mentioned by
electricity, and it is expected tnat they
will act favorably on the proposition.

LIGHT FOR THE STREETS.
Electricity Will Soon Take the Place
of Gas Lamps.
The Merchants' Association is gratified
that its efforts to improve the lighting of
the city by having electric lights in
stalled upon the business streets in place
of gas lamps have succeeded. Complying
with the recent request of the association,
the Board of Supervisors has just or
dered electric lights over the crossings of
all the business streets, in accordance
with the schedule prepared by the asso
ciation. These lights will be installed as
rapidly as possible. The electric light
company has already begun placing them
on Polk street, and the lights will be
turned on there next week.
The Merchants' Association requested
the company to install the lights on Polk
street first, as a recognition of the enter
prise of the merchants on that street,
who were the first to light their street
with electric lights, and have maintained
part of them at their own expense for
nearly two years. Assoon as the light
ing of Polk street is completed the work
of installing the lights downtown will
commence, and the lamps will be lighted
on each street as soon as all the lamps
on that street are installed.
Trouble Over a Chinese.
The Chinese Bureau Is in a quandary
over the case of Ginn Ken Yin, who ar
rived here on the Zealandia on May 28.
He applied for admission to the coun
try on the claim that he was born here,
but it was proven that he was born in
China, and Commissioner Heacock or
dered he be sent bark to China. When
the time came to deport him the Pa
cific Mail Steamship Company refused to
carry him, as they had not brought him
bt-re, and it is impossible to send him
to Honolulu, as he was ordered to China.
The case will probably be taken to the
United States courts to have the kinks
straightened out.

THE SAN FRANCISCO CALX., FRIDAY, AUGUST 26, 1898.
FIRE CHIEF'S
ANNUAL REPORT
More Engines Should Be
Provided.
OTHER RECOMMENDATIONS
FULLY PAID DEPARTMENT IS
STRONGLY ADVOCATED.
Water Supply Should Be Increased
by the Removal of Small Mains
and Larger Ones Sub
stituted.
At the meeting of the Fire Commis
sioners yesterday afternoon Chief Sulli
van submitted his annual report, which
contained some valuable information.
The appropriation for the past fiscal
year was $62Â»,000 and the expenditure
$625,975 10, leaving a balance of $24 90. The
total losses by tire amounted to $878,
259 72, the total Insurance on the prop
erty $4,317,721 76, and the total amount
paid on insurance $673,786 72. There had
been 911 alarms of fire, including still
alarms, and sixteen and four-sixths miles
of pipe had been laid.
The chief made the following recom
mendations:
I renew my recommendation that the de
partment be reorganized Into a fully paid sys
tem, with the entire force continually on duty.
The force should be increased to at least thirty
five steam lire engine companies, nine truck
companies, six chemical engine companies, two
water tower companies, one nreboat company
and two monitor battery companies.
The engine-houses now occupied by engine
company 22 and truck 5 ehould be torn down
unJ a new modern structure erected in
its place. A Bultable lot should be purchased
in the vicinity of Point L*>bos and Masonic
avenues and a new engine-house built thereon,
and engine company 22 transferred there from
its present location on Post street, near Fill
more, as said district Is in need of better pro
tection. A lot and house should be purchased
and erected in the vicinity of tuxth and Bran-
I nan streets and a new truck company stationed
thereat Tins in in the heart of the milling dis
trict, where numerous fires have demonstrated
the necessity of better protection. I further
recommend the purchase of a suitable lot and
the f-rection of a new building for the water
In view of the immense value of shipping
interests of this city better protection should
be glvi-n tn the water front, and I therefore
earnestly recommend that a light-draught,
high-power nreboat of good speed and large
pumping capacity be provided for that pur
pose Hundreds of thousands of dollars might
be saved to the community by tfce expenditure
I of comparatively few thousands in this direc-
A new steel drill tower should be built in tha
rear of enÂ«lne-houÂ«e 28. All en*lne-bpOMi
should be lighted by electricity and the latest
appliances employed for that purpose. The
; silent system of signals and new switch boards
â  should be provided in all firehouses of the de
i partment. The latest improved keyless doors
! should be placed on the fire boxes in the raer
j cantilf districts, as a means" of saving much
i valuable time in turning In alarms of fire.
I again recommend the immediate removal
i of the fire alarm office to pome suitable place
1 in the City Hall, and the purchase of a proper
â  plant for the conduct of this important branch
; of the Fire Department. In its present location
the office Is in great danger of being destroyed
I by fire, together with its valuable appliances,
, and should be located in a safer position.
I also recommend that the water supply
throughout the city bt- increased by the re
moval of all small and inadequate water mains
and replacing them with eight, twelve, sixteen
an<] twenty-four inch mains in all portions of
the city not already supplied with mains of
large and sufficient capacity, and more fire
liy.lr.ints ph^uld be set in all districts that are
not sufficiently provided therewith. I further
recommend that all cisterns now available for
fire purposes be repaired, and that 100 addl
i tlonal underground reservoirs, with capacities
of not less than 10,000 gallons, be built on the
I corners of streets in thf> hotel and mercantile
: districts, and fed by eight-Inch pipes with gate
I valves attached directly from the street mains.
| By this means many more engines could be
! stationed nearer to fires, nnd their streams
advanced into one large stream would enable
I the department to fight fires more effectively.
REPORTS FROM RAILROADS.
One Road Shows a Gain for the Year
and Another a Large Deficit.
In accordance with the law, the Nevada,
California and Oregon Railway filed its
annual report for the fiscal year ending
June 30, 1898. with the Railroad Commis
sioners yesterday. This company operates
si-vi nty-nlne mites of track, running be
tween Reno, Nevada, and Amedee, Cali
fornia. The report shows that the gross
earnings were $84,738 05 and the operating
expenses $05,069 *'â <. leaving a net income
for the year of $19,663 22.
The Areata and Mad River Railroad,
which is eighteen miles in length, also sub
mitted a statement which shows their
gross earnings to be $39,082 99. and the
operating expenses $78,662 08, thus leaving
a deficit of $39,57:) 09.

Valley Road Prepress.
Connection will soon be made on the
San Joaquin Valley road between Visalia
and Tulare, the track layers having
reached a point just one mile from the
latter place. It Is expected that all work
will be finished by Saturday next, but
no trains will be run on this branch of
the road until the fourteen miles of track
between Tulare and Corcoran Junction
will have been completed, which will be
by the end of next month.
Work on the tunnel through the Contra
Costa hills for the extension west from
Stockton to Point Richmond is pro
gressing as rapidly as the tedious nature
of that work will allow. Of the 5700 feet
to be bored, the east end is In nearly
500 feet and the west end 700 feet, thus
making a total of 1200 feet, or about one
fifth of the entire length.
General Fitzhugh Lee tells about
Cuba's political future in next Sun
day's Call.
Fell Dead on the Street.
Hermann C. Sieb, a well-known musi
cian, dropped dead on Leavenworth street
yesterday afternoon. He has been suffer
ing from epilepsy for some time and this
is supposed to have caused his death. He
j was married and lived with his family
at 112 Leavenworth ptreet.
