A PBOIHSINa FUTURE.
A WELL-KNOWN AUTHORITY PREDICTS
A FINE FALL TRADE.
The cncular of Henry Clews, under date of
New York, August -' . says: "The return of
peace the promise of business prosperity and
en abundance of cheap money have been ir
resistible factors of the Stock Exchange. Of
course many of the sensational advances of
this week can be attributed to manipulation,
but such manipulation would not have been
possible did not the favorable conditions exist.
As a matter of fact there was much less ma
nipulation than usual in a market of the recent
magnitude and strength. Professional operators
saw the market taken right away from the
by the rush of outside buying, and all *Â«<Â£"
to check the advance Â«"Â« fruitless for a time
day's rise would have marked the culm nation
of a bull movement, but this can hardly be co
new. although no surprise need be exprtsbed
If the pace has been shown too rapid and i
resting *peil U. needed. Ac we have, however,
repeatedly stated these are not ordinary times
[he ccndttioM Â«ue very unusual and one-sided
and it iÂ« many yew.) since we have had each
a complex absence of disturbing conditions.
The putting of Burlington and Qulncy . upon a
6 per cent basis was a very practu-ai l 11 "? 1 â¢"
lion of the improvement In the railroad situa
tion. A lew roads have already increased
their dividends and others are sure to follow.
Oa a i per cent basis an advance of 1 per cent
In the dividend rate ought to raise the value
or an ordinary stock at least 20 points, so .that
the prospect of Increased dividends affords a
bail* for the strongest and most active kind
Â°'-Th;-'war : "i"no longer a factor affecting the
interests that center in Wall street. Its ex
citing uncertainties are ended. For some time
at least the reopening of the late blockaded
ports will be attended with a return to more
than the normal movement of merchandise,
hut the matter of chief interest will be the
eager outlook for opportunities for American
Investment In plantation properties and in
mercantile undertakings. Indeed the interest
in such ventures has risen to such a pitch that
It will not be surprising if this tort of enter
prise is overdone. There can be no doubt, how
ever that under Spanish domination the
rich' sources of both Islands have never re
ceived much more than a surface development
and a large and valuable trade with botn
k-lands must soon be forthcoming.
"The future of this country is certainly full
of mlae, and all the favorable conditions
that exist stand for an active stock market
with a rising tendency. The half of the year
JS3S say from July to January 1, will beyond
doubt mark the highest notch of prosperity
which this country has attained in its history,
and it Is that prospect which Is at the bottom
of stock values at the present time. It Is not
surprising, therefore, that the current Is at
"In all my business experience In Wall street,
which covers a period of forty years, 1 nave
never seen a brighter outlook for all business
interests in this country than the situation now
presents." ,

EASTERN MARKETS.
NEW YORK STOCK MARKET.
NEW YORK. Aug. 2"i.â The close of the stock
Market v.-day gave every indication of re
newed absorption on a large scale by a pow
erful speculative interest, which accounts for
an additional rise in prices. The buying in the
la-Â«t hour followed a period of extreme dull
ness, during which the market was JÂ«ft to
itself. The tone of the trading In the earlier
part of the day showed that there was a con
siderable outside element disposed to take
pr 'fits, or to stop losses if they were
the unfortunate ones who bought stocks at the
top figures last week.
Hut it was also evident that the pressure of
this Liquidation was not severe and that the
offerings of stocks were nnt increased at the
low level of prices. On the contrary, there were
sufficient buying orders with commission
houses at a level below the market to check
the decline. Some of the recent elements of
weakness were also removed. For instance,
Manhattan showed recuperative power by ris
lng 2 points, to the distress of the bears. There
Was also sufficient vigor in the upward move
ment of sugar to give color to the rumors
prevalent of the conclusion of a working agree-
BM at with new competitors. The quarterly
statement of Lake Shore was a significant ex
hibit of the possibilities of refunding the large
decrease in nt t earnings for the present quar
ter, being so far offset by the decrease in the
charges that the surplus is still above last
year's level. The buying of New York Central
was induced by this exhibit and the buoyant
recovery of Burlington had in view the possi
bilities for that property in a refunding of Its
mortgage indebtedness. The ready response of
sterling exchange to any hardening tendency
to the .money market gives assurance of relief
from that ciuarter of any money stringency.
The special strength of the Pacifies was due
to the transference of speculative profits from
other stocks, notably St. Paul, and to a con
viction of the great benefits to accrue from the
agreement to arbitrate the trouble with the
Canadian Pacific.
There was a yielding tendency in some of the
standard railroad bonds to-day, which was
possibly a reflection of the firmer tone in
money. Low priced speculative bonds, how
ever, showed sharp advances. Total sales $3 -
275.000.
Government bonds were unchanged.
Total sales of stocks to-day were 451,401
shares, including: 4580 Atchison preferred, 13,

LONDON MARKET.
NSW YORK. Aug. 25.â The Evening Post's
London financial cablepram says: The stock
markets here were Irregular but firm to-day
on balance. Argentines and Brazils were pac
ticularly strong, the former on a further fnll
of the goid premium and the latter on Paris
buying:, the rise In exchange and the rise In
coffee. Americans opened above the parity,
but weakened later. This market presents v
tired appearance, but any material 4 reaction
is expected to bring in buyers. Prices wen
firmer In the Btreet.
Grand Trunk and Canadian Pacifies wee
good on Montreal buying. Mexican rails were
speculated in for the rise In sympathy with
silver. Spanish 4s were firm, but there were n,i
signs here to-day of any operations on New
York account. A good number of American
bills have been offered for forward delivery.
Details of the gold movement for the week
show the sale of Â£125.000 In French coin for
New York. Cuba being the ultimate destina
tion, and the receipt of Â£75,000 from Australia.

DRIED FRUITS, NUTS, RAISINS, ETC.
New York trade papers report the market for
dried fruit there dull on account of the abund
ance of fresh fruit, which deters dealers from
buying. The Commercial says: "Compara
tively little fruit is being bought on specu
lation, though the conditions Justify it. The
California crops are known to be short, and
Peaches and Apricots are failures. The can
nerÃ have taken everything possible. An in
teresting situation will probably develop later
on and prices will probably go much higher
than now thought."
C. Roemer of Los Gatos shipped the first car
of new Prunes this season yesterday. It went
to Chicago. Kather more Inquiry Is noticed for
Prunes, but it amounts to little man? than
nibbling. The other fruits stand the same.

THE STOCK MARKET.
Local securities continued active yesterday,
with numerous fluctuations. Oceanic Steamship
declined from $57 50 to $56, but recovered to
$56 75. Hawaiian Commercial sold down to
$26 874. Hana Plantation sold up to $15 50 and
Hutchinson to $56 50.
Mining stocks were unchanged.
The Hale & Norcross delinquent sales take
place to-day.
The Elkton Gold Mining Company of Colo
rado paid a dividend of 2 cents per share on
August 20.
The delinquency in office of the assessment
of 6 cents per share on Utah Consolidated
stock has been again postponed to September
13, and the (Joy of sale to October 4.
Gilbert McSf. Ross has been appointed super
intendent of the Consolidated California and
Virginia, Ophlr and Mexican mines, to fill the
vacancies caused by the resignation of D. B.
Lyman.
The official letter from the Justice mine for
the past week says: We are stoping ore south
from the winze and from the streak on the 80
foot level. The ore in the slope from the winze
shows improvement In quality. During the
past week we hoisted 9 tons of ore. The car
samples of the same averaged, gold $139 46, sil
ver $72 80; total, $212 26 per ton. The Virginia
Evening Report pays: "As soon as the second
ore bin at the Justice mine is full, work will
be suspended In the whim shaft and operations
resumed through the Woodville shaft. The sta
tion on the 370 level has been repaired and a
drift will be run to the whim or Barclay sha/t.
The Washoe mill, the property of the Justice
Company, will run on rock from the above
mine."

NOTICE TO MARINERS.
A branch of the T_*nlted States Hydrographle
Office, located In the Merchants' Exchange, la
maintained In San Francisco for the benefit of
mariners, without regard to* nationality and
free of expense.
Navigators are cordially Invited to visit the
office where complete sets of charts and Bail-
Ing directions of the world are kept on hand
for comparison and reference, and the latest
Information can always be obtained regarding
lights, dangers to navigation and all matters
of interest to ocean commerce.
The time ball on the tower of the new Ferry
building, at the foot of Market street. Is hoisted
about ten minutes before noon and dropped at
noon. 120 th meridian, by telegraphic signal re
ceived each day from the United States Signal
Observatory, Mare. Island, Cal.
A notice stating whether the ball was dropped
on time or giving the error, if any. is published
In the morning papers the following day.
CHAS. P. WELCH.
Ensign (retired). X*. S. N.. in charge.

NOTEâ In the above exposition of the tides
the early morning tides are given In the left
hand column and the successive tides of the
day in the order of occurrence as to time. The
second time column gives the second tide ot
the day, the third time column the third tide
and the last or right hand column gives the
last tide of the day. except when there are but
three tides, as sometimes occur. The heights
given are additions to the soundings on tiie
United State* Coast Survey charts, except
when a minus sign (â ) precedes the height,
and then the number given Is subtracted from
the depth given by the charts. The plane of
reference is the mean of the lower low waters.

"BILLY" VALENTINE
DIES SUDDENLY
The Children's Friend Is
No More.
HEART DISEASE THE CAUSE
Loved by the pupils of the
alameda schools.
Separated From His Wife for Years
â¢ . and Before Death Deeded
â¢ .-â -â¢" TÂ£is Property to p-
Friend.
Oakland Offloe San Francisco Call.
90S Broadwy, Aug. 25.
William Tucker Valentine, known to all
the Alameda school children asr "Grandpa
Billy," died suddenly at his home. 2252
Raiiroad avenue, Alameda, last night.
His' death was a great surprise to his
' legion of friends. He was universally
known by the young men and women
now married, who in their early days .at
tended the old Alameda School and the
High School, having had charge of the
building and grounds for the past thirty
years, and many times delighting In
:ing the youngsters In their amuse
ments.
. deceased weighed 260 pounds and his
death was due to fatty degeneration of
the heart. He was a native of Indiana
and nearly 60 years of ago. Last night
when he went to his room to retire he
laughed and joked, but before he disrobed
he screjuned for help, saying he was dy
ing; â¢ Drs. Keys a-nd Lubbock were sum
moned, but they could do nothing for
..him.
For the past twenty years deceased had
been tyler of Oak Grove Lodge No. 215,
â¢F. & A. M., under whose auspices the
. funeral will be held next Sunday after
noon. He was also a charter member of
Citizens' Hook and Ladder Truck, No. 1,
was Fire Chief in lhS3-*4. and a member
of the Exempt Firemen's Association of
California; also one of the early mem
i-.-rs of Howard Engine Company of San
Francisco.
He leaves a widow and one daughter,
Mrs. F. F. Hobbs of San Francisco. Hus
band and wife have lived apart for sev
eral years, and the property of the de
ceasedâa lot with a commodious home
â on itâ is left to his closeÃt and most inti
mate friend. Thomas J. White Jr., by
deed, which has been recorded. Mrs. Val
entine came over from San Francisco to
day and it is understood that there will
be litigation over the property, which is
valued at about $4000.
Mr. Valentine's mother was Mrs.
. Rachael Neal, who fought a bitter legal
.battle over the estate of her sister, Mrs.
Lemon, which it was claimed was ob
tained by Mrs. Mary Smith, the famous
"trumpet medium," who made use of her
powers over Mrs. Lemon to good ad
vantage.
. Deceased was a steadfast and sincere
. believer In Spiritualism and, according to
Thomas White Jr. he received a message
last Tuesday from the splritland warning
him of his approaching death. "We ware
seated in the front parlor," said Mr.
White to-day, "when all at once the
center table rose up from the floor and
came toward Billy. Of course we knew
that was a message and I inquired and
J.arned that it came from Billy's mother,
Mrs. Xeai. We did not discuss the mat
ter much, but we' both realized ihat some- :
thing was going to happen to Billy."

HARD PLAYS
IN RARD LUCK
A Justice Who Earns No
Salary in Office.
ALAMEDA'S MANY COURTS
FIVE DOLLARS A MONTH IS NOT
WORTH COLLECTING.
Next Year the Law Will Do Away
With an Office for Which
No Need Ever Ex*
isted.
Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
908 Broadway, Aug. 25.
Some surprise is shown In the Auditor's
| office that Justice Hard of Alameda
j should have any desire to continue in of-
I flee. Four months have passed away, and
j Justice Hard has not earned enough sal
ary to Justify him In filing a single state
ment. It is said that the #Uary of the
office does not produce $5 a month, yet the
Justice maintains a courtroom and is al
ways ready to do Judicial business in Ala
meda.
Under the law which will soon go into
effect the difficulty of having too many-
Judges across the estuary will be obvi
ated, as there will, be but one Justice of
the Peace. The new law declares that in
cities having a Recorder there shall not
be more than one Justice of the Peace.
Alameda now has a Recorder and
two Justices, and the salaries of all
three combined would not appear to make
the office worth seeking.
Justice Hard was appointed by the Su
pervisors several months ago. Prior to
his appointment the office had been prac
tically vacant, because the incumbent,
Judge Swasey, was lying sick at Fablola
Hospital. The income from the office for
the last fiscal year was under $40, so that
if Justice Hard be making, as is stated.
$5 a month, he is evidently doing much
more business than his predecessor.
In strong contrast to the predicament
of the Alameda Justices Is the Justice
Court of Oakland, for which the county
paid last year $4501 to the Justice and over
$3000 to the constable.

THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, FRIDAY, AUGUST 26, 1898.
CONTROLLED BY
FEAR OF DEATH
Mrs. Cook's Big Estate
Is Saved to the Heirs.
SUPREME COURT DECIDES
SHE DEEDED EVEBYTHINO TO
HEB FB.IEND WHEN SICK.
Public Administrator Knight Will
Control the Property and At
tend to Its Distri
bution.
Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
908 Broadway, Ausr. 25.
After three years of litigation the Su
preme Court has handed down a decision
which finally closes the dispute over a
vast amount of property deeded, when in
fear of death, by Mrs. Cook three years
ago to her late husband's partner, Mr.
Tripp.
The principle involved in the case, which
has twice been to the Supreme Court, la
a very interesting one, as it establishes
a precedent regarding the deeding away
of property prior to death for the purpose
of preventing nrobate proceedings.
Three years ago Mrs. Cook and her hus
band and Mr. and Mrs. Tripp came to this
city from lowa, The husbands had been
partners in business, and on their retire
ment the two families decided to end their
days in California. Cook died soon after
their arrival, and Mrs. Cook, who had no
children and had lived for years apart
from her brothers and sisters in the East,
confided her business to the Tripps. She
was suffering from a severe illness and a
capital operation was declared to be the
only hope of saving her life. Fully realiz
ing that the cuances were greatly in fa
vor of her death Mrs. Cook sent for Mr.
Tripp and gave him a statement setting
forth how she wished her estate to be
distributed. She then transferred her
bank books to him, deeded over property
In Oakland, Berkeley and Los
and also transferred mortgages on prop
erty in lowa. In addition to this she gave
him a hill of sale upon her household
furniture and all other personal effects
of which she was possessed. The entire
estate thus placed in Tripp's hands
amounted in value to $40,000, over one
fourth of which was cash.
Three days after the operation wae per
formed Mrs. Cook died. Attomev Fred
Whitney, acting for Public Administrator
Knight, ascertaining that the deceased
had lef+ no will and applied for letters of
administration. They were granted and
a demand was then made on Tripp to
turn over all the property of the deceased
woman in his possession. Tripp replied
that there was no property belonging to
Mrs. Cook's estate, as it had all been
deeded to him. Tripp was then cited to
appear in court and was forced to tell
the- whole history of the proceedings. A
replevin suit was next brought to recover
all the personal property, including the
money in bank, which had been trans
ferred to Tripp's personal account. Judge
Ogden gave a Judgment for the Adminis
trator for $11,000. Tripp argued for a new
trial, which Judge Ogden granted. The
Public Administrator appealed from the
order granting the new trial and the Su
preme Court decided against the order,
thus establishing the original Judgment.
The attorneys for Tripp succeeded in get
ting the Supreme Court to hear the ar
gument for a new trial in bank, and the
result was to deny the order and re-es
tablish the original judgment of Judge
Ogden.
Another suit is now pending to recover
the real property, valued at 130.000. The
evidence in this case is the same as for
the personal property, and the attorneys
declare that the judgment will dÂ« against
Tripp. as the Supreme Court's decision
handed down yesterday, clearly sets
forth the law in the case.
LIEUTENANT BUTT
SEVERELY CENSURED
GETS A LETTER DEFINING HIS
DUTIES.
Informed That He Does Not Own the
Port of San Francisco and Must
Obey Orders Without
Question.
Lieutenant James O. Butt, command
ing the revenue cutter Golden Gate, re
ceived a stinging letter from Washing
ton yesterday which appears to settle the
question as to whether Mr. Butt is sub
ject to orders from the customs officials
or not.
Collector Jackson, Surveyor Spear and
Chief Deputy Chauncey St. John have
had frequent passages at arms with the
commander of the cutter. They were of
the opinion that it was for the use of
the customs service, while Mr. Butt held
that It waÃ for the use of the service
subject to certain rules laid down by him,
and he accordingly took orders when the
orders suited him. This caused so many
clashes that he was finally allowed to
have his own way, but complaints were
nevertheless sent to Washington. In re
turn Lieutenant Butt received the fol
lowing letter, which he will probably
have framed and placed where all may
read it:
Treasury Department, Office of Secretary,
Washington, D. C. Aug. 16, 1898.
Lieutenant James G. Butt, R. C. S., Com
manding IT. B. S. Golden Gateâ Sir: Herewith
are transmitted four letters received from the
collector of customs at San Francisco. You
will at once submit a full explanation as to
why you have declined to comply with the
written Instructions given you under date of
April 13, 18Â»8. In thÂ« meantime and from and
after the receipt hereof you will comply with
all Instructions given you In person or In writ-
Ing by the collector of customs. If In the
future you have any doubt as to your duty un
der the laws and regulations of the revenue
cutter service, you will ask for written orders
In the premises, obey the same without ques
tion, and then. If you have a protest to make,
submit the same to the department, with a full
explanation.
You are reminded that much complaint has
been made against you by the collector because
of your manner of performing duties as com
mander of the Golden Gate. There Is no pos
sible reason for past or existing friction as
between your command and the chief customs
officer In whose district you are serving, and
you are admonished that there must be no
more of It. You, therefore, will govern your
self with a view to rendering all possible aid
to the customs authorities and you will be
careful to work in harmony with the collector
to this end. W. B. HOWELL,
Assistant Secretary.
Many instances of Lieutenant Butt's
ignoring the orders of the Collector and
Surveyor of the Port are related, one of
which is that when recently ordered to
follow a steamer outside the heads to
prevent smuggled opium being passed
from the ship to a row boat. Lieutenant
Butt refused to do so, saying that it
would do no good and waste too much
coal. As a result the vessel got away,
and it is believed that the opium which
the officers vainly searched for was
thrown overboard and picked up by
"bay pirates," who work hand in glove
with the members of the crew.

A Boy Burglar.
Raymond Swett, a 16 year old boy,
who claims} to be a wood-turner from Ar
izona, was arrested last evening and
booked for burglary and carrying bur
glar's tools. S. H. Williams, the sexton
of the First Presbyterian Church, was
reading; in tbe library when he heard a
noise in the vestibule.- On investigation
he says he found Swett attempting to
break into a money box. Swett had
in his possession a "Jimmy,' a file, a
knife and a piece of wire. He claims
that he went Into the church to rest, and
had dropped 10 cents into the box. His
parents live in Los Angeles, and he is
visiting relatives here.

Some of the con
ductors on the
Eddy street elec
tric line have a
most peculiar
way of pro
noundngr the
names of the
SHE WOULD
NOT
GET OFF.
streete when they call them out for the
Information of the passengers, and It Is
difficult to learn Just what rules of or
thography govern them In their manner
of pronounclatlon. For Instance, Post
becomes "Powst," Steiner becomes "Stee
ner." and Pine Is "Pain" In the mouth of
one word juggler. And so on with the other
streets, which are distorted In a fright
ful fashion, and cause the nerves of his
passengers to rise on edge.
Yesterday an old lady, who from her
manner was evidently deaf, paid her fare
after the car had started from Market
street, and instructed the aforesaid con
ductor to let her off at Gough street.
When the car arrived there the conduct
or had got the old lady mixed up in his :
mind with another one of Celtic persua.
â ton, and, thinking that the latter had i
made the request, he stopped the car and
addressing himself to the daughter of j
Erin he said, "Go-off street!" She made
no response, and the deaf lady did not
hear the name of the street at which she
wished to alight, or else did not under- !
stand it as pronounced. Fixing a stern
look of offended dignity on the woman
for delaying the car, he repeated In a |
higher key, "Go-off street!" She now
realized that he was addressing her, and, I
arranging herself as If to take a firmer '.
grip on her seat, she gazed at the ex
asperated conductor and said In decided
accents, "I'll not go off. I've paid my
fare, and I am going to ride to the end
of the line." By that time the deaf lady
had made out that it was her street, and
she left the car. which proceeded on its
Journey, and the determined Celtic lady
went with it.

CORBETT WILL SOON LEAVE.
The Ex-Champion Is Anxious to Get
Back East â McConnell and
Green Will Be Matched.
Jim Corbett will leave for New York on
Monday. The ex-champion cannot say
what his plans will be when he will ar
rive in the big Eastern city, but he will
doubtless make some arrangements "with
McCoy with a view of holding their pros
pective fight in the near future. ( Corbett
says that McCoy acted very manfully
concerning the postponement of their en
gagement, but as it is purely a matter
of business with him, he will try and
have the contest carried off according to
the original plans, excepting that the
time of the fight will necessarily be
changed to a later date. ,
/ The National Club signed Frank Me-
Connell last evening to fight George
Green in this city some time next month.
McConnell stipulates, however, : that
Green must - not weigh over 152 pounds,
and that if he should fail to meet that
weight when he weighs in he will have
to forfeit 75 per cent of the fighters 1 por
tion of the proceeds. Green could not
be found last evening, but as he ex
pressed great anxiety to fight any man
in his class, doubtless he will agree to
McConnell's terms, and that will mean
a warm contest between ; two clever fel
lows In the near future.

MRS. QTJACKENBUSH'S GLAjMS
Her Case Concluded and the Defense
Opens Monday.
The case of the plaintiff in the action of
Mrs. Carlotta Quackenbush to recover
possession of half the estate of Thomas
M. Quackenbush was concluded yesterday
afternoon, and the defendant's case will
go on next Monday.
Jerry McCarthy, a liquor-dearer, wag the
most important witness for the plaintiff
yesterday. He testified that he was a po
lice officer for several years, and arrested
Thomas Quackenbush, a son of the de
ceased capitalist, in IWW on a. charge of
murder. The young man at that time
went by the name of Dawson, and when
McCarthy informed the father of the son s
arrest he asked him how It was that the
young man had repudiated his na jn e :
Quackenbush answered that he had had
some difficulty with his wife, and that she
left him and took the name of Dawson.
The son did the same. Other witnesses
were put on the stand and the case of the
plaintiff completed. The defendant ex
pects to break down the showing of the
plaintiff without difficulty, and many wit
nesses have been summoned to appear.
KISSED AND MADE TJP.
William Walker, the Klondike Min
er, and His Wife Reunited.
William Walker, the Klondike miner,
and his wife are reconciled again,' al
though he wanted her punished Wednes
day night for leaving him and taking
with her $10,000 worth of deeds to Alaska
mines and $3600 in gold.
Mrs. Walker was found In the Galindo
Hotel, Oakland, yesterday morning by
CASTORIA
For Infanta and Children, .:
TUtio- rf _
Detective Anthony and her husband,
where she had registered as "N. L.
Davis." She delivered over the deeds
and the money to the detective, who j
brought her across the bay to police l
headquarters. Walker and his wife later
agreed to kiss and make up, and after j
securing the deeds and money from the
police they left the City Hall together.
Bussell J. Wilson Stricken.
Mr. Russell J. Wilson, the well-known
attorney and member of the board of di
rectory of the Southern Pacific Company,
was stricken with apoplexy Wednesday
evening while eating luncheon at the Pa
cific Union Club, in company with Her
man Oelricha, Daniel T. Murphy and sev
eral other gentleman. He gesticulated
wildly and then fell unconscious from the
table. Dr. C. M. Richter and Dr. Moffltt
rendered assistance and within fifteen
minutes the sufferer recovered. He was
taken to his home on California street
and last evening was reported greatly im
proved. It is expected that he will be
around again within a few days.

PROCLAMATION.
STATE OF CALIFORNIA.
EXECUTIVE DEPARTMENT.
SACRAMENTO, July 30th, 1898.
WHEREAS, The Legislature of the State of
California, at its thirty-second session begin-
ning on the fourth day of January, A. D. 1897,
two-thirds of all the members elected to each
of the two houses of said Legislature voting
in favor thereof, proposed the following de-
scribed amendments to the Constitution of the
State of California, to-wlt:
AMENDMENT NUMBER ONE.
(Belne Senate Constitutional Amendment No.
*1.)
A resolution to propose to the people of the
State of California an amendment to section
eighteen of article eleven of the Constitu-
tion, in relation to revenue and taxation, by
which it is proposed to amend said section to
read as follows:
Bectlon 18. No county, city, town, township,
board of education or school district shall in-
cur any indebtedness or liability in any man-
ner or for any purpose exceeding In any year
the Income and revenue provided for it for
such year, without the assent of two-thirds
of the qualified electors thereof, voting at an
election to be held for that purpose, nor un-
less before, or at the time of incurring such
Indebtedness, provision shall be made for the
collection of an annual tax sufficient to pay
the interest on such Indebtedness as It falls
due and also provision to constitute a sinking
fund for the payment of the principal thereof
on dr before maturity, which shall not exceed
forty years from the time of contracting the
came; provided, however, that the City and
County of San Francisco may at any time pay
the unpaid claims with interest thereon for
materials furnished to and work done for said
city and county during the forty-third and
forty-fourth fiscal years, out of the Income and
revenue of any succeeding year or years; pro-
vided that any and all claims for making, re-
pairing, altering or for any work done upon or
for any material furnished for any street, lane,
alley, court, place or sidewalk, or for the con-
struction of any sewer or sewers in said city
and county are hereby cxcepted from the pro-
visions of this section; and in determining any
claim permitted to be paid by this section, no
statute of limitations shall apply in any man-
ner; and provided further, that the City of
Vallejo in Solano County, may pay its exist-
ing indebtedness incurred in the construction
of Its water works, whenever two-thirds of the
electors thereof voting at an election held for
that purpose shall so decide. Any indebted-
ness or liability incurred contrary to this pro-
vision with the exception hereinbefore recited,
shall be void.
AMENDME7*T NUMBER TWO.
(Being Senate Constitutional Amendment No.
A resolution proposing to the people of. the
State of California an amendment to tnÂ« Con-
stitution of the State, by adding a new sec-
tion to be known and designated as section
i seven and one-haH. article eleven thereof, pro-
vldlng ter the framing by the Inhabitant* of
counties of local county government acts for
their own government. The said proposed new
section to read as follows:
Section 7%. The Inhabitants of any county
I may frame a county government act for their
own government, relating to the matters here-
inafter specified, and consistent with and sub-
ject to the Constitution and laws of this State,
by causing a board of fifteen freeholders, who
have been, for at least five years, qualified
electors of such county, to be elected by the
qualified electors of such county, at any gen-
eral or special election, whose duty It shall be
within ninety days after such election, to pre-
pare and propose a county government act for
such county, which shall be signed In dupli-
cate by the members of such board, or a ma-
jority of them, and returned, one copy thereof
to the Board of Supervisors or other legisla-
tive body of such county, and the other copy
to be sent to the Recorder of Deeds of the
county Such proposed county government act
shall then be published in two papers of gen-
eral circulation in such county, or if there be
not two such papers, then In one only, for at
least twenty days, and within not less than
thirty days after such publication it shall be
submitted to the qualified electors of such
county at a general or special election, and If
a majority of such qualified electors voting
thereon shall ratify the same. It shall there-
after be submitted to the Legislature for Its
rejection or approval, as a whole, without
power of alteration or amendment, and If ap-
proved by a majority of the members elected
to each house, it shall be the county govern-
ment act of such county, and shall In such
rase become the organic law thereof and super-
sede any existing county government act, and
all amendments thereof, and all special laws
Inconsistent with such county government act.
A copy of such county government act, certi-
fied by the President of the Board of Super-
visors or other legislative body of such county,
and authenticated by the seal of such county.
setting forth the submission of such county
government act to the electors, and its ratifica-
tion by them, shall be made in duplicate and
deposited, one In the office of the Secretary of
State the other, after being recorded in the
office' of the Recorder of Deeds In the county,
among the archives of the county
All courts shall take Judicial notice thereof.
The county government act so ratified may be
amended at Intervals of not less than two
years by proposals therefor, submitted by the
legislative authority of the county, to the
qualified electors thereof, at a general or E pe-
clal election held at least forty days after the
publication of such proposals for twenty days
In a newspaper of general circulation In such
count" Tnd ratified by at least three-fifths of
the qualified electors voting thereon, and ap-
proved by the Legislature as herein provided
for the approval of the county government act.
In submitting any such county government act
any alternative article or proposition may be
presented for the choice of the voters, and may
Ye voted on separately without prejudice to
Ot It er shall be competent In all county govern-
ment acts framed under the authority given by
this section, to provide for the manner in
which the times at which, and the terms for
which 'the several township and county officers
rther than Judges of the Superior Court, shall
be elected or appointed: for their compensa-
tion: for the number of such officers for the
consolidation or segregation of offices, for the
number of deputies that each officer .hall have
ond for the compensation payable to each of
inch deputies, for the manner In which, the
time* 1n which, and the terms for which the
numbers of all boards of election shall be
elected or appointed and for the constitution,
regulation compensation and government of
such boards and of their clerks and attaches:
alÂ«o to prescribe the manner and method by
which all elections by the people shall be con-

pate In any primary election.
Whenever any county has. In the manner
and method herein pointed out adopted nnv
county government act. and the same shall
have been approved by the Legislature as
aforesaid the direction of sections four and
flve of this article providing for the uniformity

| Section 15. A Lieutenant Governor shall be
! elected at the same time and place and in the

| office and his qualifications shall be the same.
He shall be president of the Senate, but shall

Section 18. In case of the Impeachment of
the Governor, or his removal from office, death.
Inability to discharge the powers and duties of

State, the powers and duties of the office shall
devolve upon the Lieutenant Governor for the

impeached, displaced, resign, die or become In-

tempore of the Senate shall act as Governor un-
til the vacancy In the office of Governor shall
be filled at the next general election when
members of the Legislature shall be chosen, or
until such disability of the Lieutenant Gov-
ernor shall cease. In case of a vacancy in the
office of Governor for any of the reasons above
named, and neither the Lieutenant Governor
nor the president pro tempore of the Senate
ehall succeed to the powers and duties of Gov-
ernor, then the powers a*d duties of such of-
fice shall devolve upon the speaker of the As-
sembly, until the office of Governor shall be
filled at such general election.

A resolution to propose to the people of the
State of California an amendment to section
six, article nine of the Constitution of the State
of California, relating to grammar schools, by
which It le proposed to amend said section to
read as follows:
Section 6. The public school system shall In-
clude primary and grammar schools, and such
high schools, evening schools, normal schools
and technical schools as may be established by
the Legislature or by municipal or district
authority, but the entire revenue derived from
the State school fund and the State school tax
shall be applied exclusively to the support of
primary and grammar schools. Grammar
schools shall Include schools organized in a
school district, or union of school districts,
having more than one thousand Inhabitants, in
which a course of study shall be taught which
will prepare pupils to enter the agricultural,
mlnl-ng or scientific department of the Univer-
sity of California. â¢

A GUARD FOR THE TREASURE
A SCANDAL IN THE TWENTIETH
EANSAS BEGIMENT.
Eighth California Will Make Its
First Appearance To-Morrow
in a Drill for the Chris
tian Commission.
In reply to a telegraphic inquiry as to
â¢what should be done with the remaining
two and a half companies of the First
New York Regiment at the Presidio, a
dispatch was received from the "War De
partment yesterday ordering the detach
ment to be forwarded to Honolulu on the
Seandia. which sails to-morrow morning
for Manila. The New Workers, under the
command of Lieutenant Colonel Stacpole,
have accordingly been ordered to embark
to-day not later than 3 o'clock, and It Is
expected that they will break camp this
morning.
M.ijor Frank S. Rice, commanding the
First Battalion of Heavy Artillery, Cali
fornia Volunteers, has been ordered to
send to the Seandia this morning a de
tachment of one lieutenant and twenty
rnen to act as a guard over the $1.
000,<XX) now on board and consigned to tne
paymaster's department at Manila. This
guard, which relieves the guard of the
.Twenty-third Infantry, goes through to
the Philippines. Lieutenant A. P. Hayne
been assigned to the command of the
treasure guard. Major S_chofield, paymas
ter, is In charge of the money.
The Seandia will take IT7 members of
the Hospital Corps and four Red Cross
nurses for Manila.
Major Kobe of the Third Artillery, Lieu
tenant "Wedgwood of Battery B, Utah Ar
tillery, and Lieutenant Wright of the
Fourteenth Infantry will also Pail on tne
Seandia to join their commands.
THE EIGHTH CALIFORNIA.
Will March Up Market Street
To- Morrow Afternoon and
Drill at Recreation Park.
The Eighth California Regiment, in I
command of Colonel Park Henshaw, will j
make Its flrst appearance in San Fran-
Cisco to-morrow afternoon, and that it !
will create a most favorable impression
there is not the least doubt, as it is â 
finely equipped and thoroughly drilled. It !
is composed of some of the best material
In Northern California. That the boys j
â will be given an enthusiastic reception is j
an assured fact.
At noon the regiment will arrive at the j
ferry from Oakland and march up Mar- !
ket street to Sixth, down Sixth to Fol- \
som and out Folsom to Recreation Park,
at Sixteenth street, where a drill will be i
given, beginning at 2:30. The programme |
arranged by Lieutenant Colonel Carring
ton embraces many Interesting features i
and maneuvers, among which is a street I
riot drill by the Third Battalion, Major
Forbes commanding. Captain Simpson's
Company <J of Alamcda will have an im
portant part, as will also Company C,
cnmmaniiHii by Captain Barnes. Nearly
the entire regiment will participate. A
review stand has been erected, and Gen
eral Miller and staff will review the regi
ment. All the colonels and staffs ot the
various commands will be in attendance;
also Governor Budd and staff. Music will
be furnished by Hie First Tennessee,
Twentieth Kansas and Eighth California
regimental bands. There will be ample
accommodations in the driveway for car
riages for all who df-sire to come in their
own conveyances. All who attend are re
quested to bring small American flags.
A SERIOUS CHARGE.
Made by the Embezzling Com-
missary of the Twentieth
Kansas Regiment.
Commissary Sergeant James A. Young
of the Twentieth Kansas Raiment, who
was tried by a court-martial and con
victed of tho charge of embezzling com
missary stores and sentenced to one year's
imprisonment in the penitentiary at Fort
Leavenwortb. made his first statement
concerning the case yesterday, and if
what lie says ho true there has been an
awful miscarriage of military Justice com
mitted by the court that tried him.
Young claims that he disposed of the
commissary stores with the knowledge of
the officers and men of the regiment and
that the total .sum was invested in to
bacco for th? regiment. This he sold to
the officers and companies, each captain
being responsible for his company. Young
lias a statement prepared from his ac
counts, which shows that, with one ex
ception, no officer paid for any tobacco
purchased, and none of thÂ» companies
paid anything at all. Captain Hamilton
paid Young for the tobacco he ought
from him. and this Young says he has in
his possession, ready to turn over. The
amounts charged against the officers and
companies is $139 4.">, and Young's state
ments account for every cent of this. The
sergeant hints at a combination that was
at work in the regiment to ruin him. and
intimates that it so affected the court that
no effort was made to investigate the cor
rectnesses of his accounts or his state
ments of the affair.. He declares that the
blamf has not been settled upon the
proper person.
Events at the Presidio.
The Dewfl of the intended mustering out
of the volunteer regiments causes very
little disappointment amonc the men of
the regiments at the Presidio. As they
are not to see active service they are all
anxious to go home. In the opinion of
the best informed officers at the Presidio
the troops now mobilized upon this coast,
with the exception of the First Washing
ton, will be the last to be mustered out.
They declare thai from a point of econ
omy the (Jovernment cannot afford to
muster these regiments out until the
peace question ha.s been formally dis
posed of and the perplexing problem of
Hawaii solved. If troops shoum for some
reason be suddenly roc4Uired in hte Phil
ippines tuose now here would be hte most
readily available, and it is cheaper to
keep them here for a time than It would
be to bring them from more distant
points.
The Third Battalion of the Twenty
ihird Infantry and the Oregon recruits
attached will move over from Camp Mer
ritt to-day to the Presidio. This means
the last of the pest hole. The subsist
ence depot on Point Lobos avenue will
remain ther^ for the present.
There were no new cases of typhoid
fever sent to the Division Hospital yes
terday from the Seventh California Reg
iment. Major Choate said that of the
sixteen cases now there eleven are sus
pects, and he is unable to determine as
vet whether they are typhoid or some
other kind of fever. Two of the total
are malarial fever, and all of them were
getting along nicely yesterday.
Private Lynch. Company X, First
Washington Regiment, now lies in the
Post Hospital at the point of death. His
spine is injured find his intestines rup
tured as a result of falling down the
stairs in the barracks where his com.
I>any is quartered.
Private Anderson of the First Tennes
see was before the general court-martial
yesterday to be tried for having been
implicated in tifc attack upon Dan
Thomas. A number of men from the
Fifty-first lowa Regiment were exam
ined. The charge against Private
Scruggs of striking Thomas over the head
when he was in the custody of an officer
was quashed, as the charges failed to
Bpeclfy the time and place of the assault.
The court will be in session again to
day.

A MILE OF DEEP-WATER SHIPS.
There are more deep water vessels in the bay just now than there has been for months past. Five ships and
two barks arrived from foreign ports Wednesday night and yesterday, and nearly all anchored in the
stream to the north of the ferries. The ships Mayneld, E. Baggio, Thornliebank and Duchalburn are
coal-laden, the barks Inversnaid and Inverurie also have black diamonds aboard, while the barken tine
Archer is sugar laden from Honolulu. These, with the ship Santa Clara from Kodiak, make the biggest
arrival of deep water vessels within twenty-four hours this year.

and a detail from that Kansas regiment.

Press Club Officers.
The annual election of tne Press Club
of San Francisco was held at the club
rooms on Ellis street yesterday. Unusual
importance attached to the election be
cause of the fact that it was the first
held in the line new Press Club building,
and upon the new board of managers
will devolve considerable responsibility
in the way of making the cozy quarters
more attractive and adding to the popu
larity of this social organization. The
Press Club is now "on Easy street" in
a financial way, has pretty and well ap
pointed quarters and the outlook for the
future is gratifying.
In yesterday's election the contests were
close in every instance. The officers and
directors chosen for the ensuing year are

net.

George B. Fraser.

CHARGED WITH MURDERING A BABE.
JOSEPH ROSSI, a Bootblack, Who, in a Fight With G. Annuzzi,
Accidentally injured a Child.
Joseph Rossi, a bootblack at Third and Mission streets, was arrested
at an early hour yesterday morning, and later was booked at the City
Prison on the charge of murdering a baby, 13 months old, the son of G.
Annuzzt, a bootblack living at 171 South Park.
Rossi had purchased the bootblack stand from Annuzzi about two
months ago, and Annuzzi soon afterward made overtures to Rossi to get
back the stand without success. On the night of August S Annuzzi, car
rying his baby boy In his arms, was passing the stand and he stopped to
talk with Rossi. A quarrel ensued and Annuzzi- drew a razor out of his
pocket and Slashed Rossi on the shoulder. Rossi grabbed one of the feet
rests and nrade a swipe at Annuzzi, missing him and striking the babe on
the base of the skull. Charles McCarthy, 735 Mission street, saw the brawl
and rushed In between the combatants, receiving a cut on the hand from
Annuzzi's razor.
Annuzzi was arrested by Policemen Tyrrell and T. C. Murphy and
booked at the City Prison on two charges of assault with a deadly weapon.
He was released on bonds the following day. On August 15 Annuzzi had
Rossi arrested for assault with a deadly weapon, as Dr. ISercndt, who had
been called in to attend the baby, expressed the opinion that it was suffer
ing from a fracture at the base of the skull and might die. These cases
are still pending in court. ,
The baby became unconscious and four other physicians were sum
moned by the anxious parents to consult with Dr. Berendt, but their com
bined skill was unavailing and the baby died early .yesterday morning.
The police were notified and Rossi, who was out on bail, was arrested.
The body of the babe was taken to the Morgue and Dr. Gallagher
made an autopsy on it and be found that death was due to pneumonia
and not to a fractured skull. He found a contusion at the base of the
skull, but no fracture. It Is probable that when Dr. Gallagher gives his
evidence in court the charge of murder will be withdrawn.
TIIE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, FRIDAY, AUGUST 26, 1898.
BLAME WILL
NOW BE FIXED
Inqueston Mrs. Paxton's
Remains To-Day.
RECOVERED FROM THE BAY
MISFORTUNE ATTENDED THE
DAWSON CITY.
Ban Out of Coal in Midocean, Next
She Broke Down, and Finally
Was Mistaken for a Span
ish Pirate.
The remains of Mrs. Charles E. Paxton
were recovered from the bay yesterday
morning. The body came to the surface
off Mission-street wharf, and, therefore,
had not moved a great distance from
where the Scandia's launch went down
last Wednesday week. The launch, it will
be remembered, was backing out from the
slip between Broadway and Pacific street
wharves when the Government steamer
McDowell, on her way from Alcatraz,
struck and sunk her. The supposition is
that the undertow, caused by the com
ing and going of the ferry steamers, car
ried the body along in a southerly direc
tion.
Early yesterday morning the mate ot
the American ship Santa Clara engaged
George Price, a. boatman, to take him
out to his vessel, which was lying in the
stream. When midway between the Mis
sion and Howard-street wharves they
saw the body of a woman floating face
upward in the bay. After making a rope
fast to the remains, Price towed it to
the Folsom-street float and notified the
Coroner. All the unfortunate woman's
jewelry was intact with the exception of
her earrings. The crabs and fish had
destroyed the lobes of her ears, and, of
course, the earrings disappeared.
The inquest over the remains will be
heard this morning, and the chances are
that Some insight will be obtained into
the cause of the disaster. Among those
who have been subpoenaed are Mate
Peter Grimm and Deckhands Jacob Jen
sen and Alec Riehter of the McDowell,
and Captain Hart. Mate Thompson and
Nat Messer Jr. of the Scandia's party,
and Captain Richardson of the steamer
Sunol.
Mrs. Paxton's body was, by tho courtesy
of the Coroner, removed to Gray's under
taking parlors ajid will be cremated to
day.
The Oceanic Steamship Company's
Moana arrived from Sydney, Auckland,
Apia and Honolulu last Wednesday night
with 117 cabin and 110 steerage passen
gers. She was late getting in. owing to
having been caught in a heavy storm
between Sydney and Auckland, and also
through the breaking of a water main in
Honolulu, which delayed her in getting
water. The Moana was six days and one
hour from Honolulu, beating the Belgic'a
time by forty-three minutes. The Moana
brought up |1,660,000 in treasure from
Australia, representing the balance of
trade In favor of the United States.
The Moana brings the news that the
schooner Wing and Wing from San Fran
cisco arrived at Apia, Samoa, July t %,
and sailed again for this port on Au
gust 5.
The gold hunters who went north from
here on the steamer Dawson City ha% r e
had a hard time of it. Their vessel was not
what she was cracked up to be and
never fulfilled the expectations of her
trial trip. At no time did she develop
any speed, and it took thirty-five days
to make a voyoge that ought to have
been covered in ten or twelve days. A
private letter from one of the passen
gers, dated at Dutch Harbor, says the
rudder is too small, and the vessel
almost impossible to keep on her course
in consequence. When fourteen days out
from San Francisco the coal was all
burned up, and for seven days every
body aboard was wondering whether or
not they would ever see land again.
After a great deal of maneuvering
enough sail was got on the Dawson City
to give her steerage way, and finally Ko
diak Island was made. All hands and
the cook were sent ashore to cut fire
wood, and after some hard work suffi
cient was obtained to carry th" steamer
to Wood Island, where a supply of coal
and water was obtained from the North
American Trading and Transportation
Company's agc-nt.
Three days out from Wood Island the
Dawson City broke down, and as a storm
was coming up, the captain decided to
put into Halibut bay on Walibut Island.
This spot is leased by the McCollom
Fishing and Trading Company, and the
fislK-rmen. having beard of the war, at
(â net- sÂ«t tho gold hunters down for â 
band -if Spanish pirates. When they at
tempted to land they were met by an
armed force, and Messrs. Rosendorg and
ftiockel were in such a hurry to get back
to the Dawson City that they capsized
their boat and were nearly drowned. It
was only by displaying Old Glory that
tho fishermen realized that it was their
countrymen they were attacking. Ros
endorg' n:id Mockel swiim to a rock, and
it was only after a great deal of trouble
and at some personal risk that Captain
Bruce got them off.
After repairing damages ana laying in
a stock or halibut the Dawson City sailed
for Dutch Harbor, which <.y;.p finally
reached, thirty-five days out from San
Francisco. The company on the steam
er does not expect to do any mining, but
will dredge the many bars in the Yukon
Ri\>r for gold. â  '
Tho transports Australia. City of Syd
ney and City of Peking are going back
on* their old runs. The Sydney is being
got ready for a voyage to Panama, the
Australia for Honolulu and the Peking;
for China. Tho other transports as they \
arrive will be returned to their various
owners.
E. L. Colnon, president of the State
Hoard of Harbor Commissioners, re
turned from a trip to Puget Sound yes
terday. He sailed from here on the ship
K. p. Rice and was down on the articles
as an "A. B." He came back on the,
Walla Walla as n cabin passenger. Life
before the mast evidently did not agree [
with him. Mr. Colnon says he had a
most enjoyable trip up tho coast on the
Rico and returns to work feeling ten
years younger for his outing.
George 1... Rowland, the chief engineer
of the steamer Emma. Is back from a
hunting trip. His friends will be revel
ing in venison and bear steaks for a
week to come, as George's trusty Win
chester brought down eight full grown
bucks and two cinnamon bears. Mr.
Rowland is a great pedestrian, but
nevertheless the tramping after those
eight door was a little too much for him
and yesterday he went to Southern Cali
fornia to recuperate.
The Alaska Packers Association has
been given the use of Folsom street
wharf for the season. The salmon pack
Is expected to be a good one this season
and tho bulk of it will be handled at
that wharf. The ship Santa Clara, the
first of tho Salmon fleet, arrived Wed
nesday night and docked at Folsom
street wharf yesterday.

Knowing her hysterical condition and

cused woman. I have no occasion to

But it would be unprofessional and out

was suffering from hysteria and nervous
prostration. The prescription which I

"Hysterical women sometimes talk a
great deal, do they not. doctor?"
"Yes. but remember this has no bearing
upon the condition of Mrs. Botkin. Fur
ther than this I have nothing to say."
The late Dr. Luke Robinson was Mrs.
Botkin's physician for many months, and
it is said he probably knew more of her
temperament and characteristics than
any other man.

THE SAIST FRANCISCO CALL, FRIDAY, AUGUST 26, 1898.
THE ANONYMOUS LETTERS IDENTIFIED
A Strong Link in the Evidence That Is Being Forged
Aronnd the Defiant Woman in the City Prison.
DOVER, Del., Aug. 25.â Attorney General "White to-day talked freely
on the main evidence in his possession of Mrs. Botkin's guilt. Among
other things he divulged was an important link in John P. Dunning's
statement to him, which had hitherto been a secret jealously guardedâ
Dunning's reason for picking Mrs. Botkin from the many women with
whom he had intimate relations in San Francisco as the one that unques
tionably sent the box of poisoned chocolates which killed his wife and his
sister-in-law.
"Dunning," said the Attorney General, "made a clean breast of every
thing to me. He held nothing back. He was as sincere as he could
be in his desire to bring the fiendish murderess to justice. The moment
he was shown the anonymous letters that were sent from San Fran
cisco to Mrs. Dunning, telling her that her husband was paying promis
cuous attention to women there, he recognized the handwriting and
Â§aid it was that of Mrs. Botkin. We had already been on her trail and
that statement from Dunning made us doubly confident, for it was the
first piece of sure evidence we had obtained against her. In a like man
ner Dunning identified the writing on the wrapper of the box and note
inclosed. He had received many letters from her, letters so teeming
with love and terms of affection that he could never forget the hand
writing. Unfortunately, he did not have any of Mrs. Botkin's letters
to him in his possession at the time, all of them having been destroyed.
He expects that when his delayed mail is forwarded to him from Porto
Rico it will contain several of her letters. These he will turn over
for use In the trial."
CLAIM TO HAVE
STRONG EVIDENCE
A Fear in Dover That Mrs.
Botkin Will Commit Sui
cide.
DOVER, Del., Aug. 25.â Attorney Gen
eral White. to-night sent to San
Francisco the requisition papers for Mrs.
Botkin, who Is under arrest there,
charged with the poisoning of Mrs. Dun
ning and Airs. Define by means of candy
sent through the mail.
The affidavit to secure the papers was
sworn to by ex-Congressman Pennlng
ton, the father of the two poisoned
women, and the papers charge Mrs. Bot
kin with murder in the first degree.
Before leaving here for Georgetown to
night Attorney General White said:
1 "We have the murderess and we have
evidence that no defense can shatter. Tne
only thing we fear is Mrs. Botkin's, seÂ«-
destruction."
It is stated here that Detective Witsil
and a police matron will go West to
bring Mrs. Botkln here. The California
witnesses will not be summoned to Dela
ware until the trial is called, but their
affidavits will be taken at San Francisco.
The trial will probably bo called in Oc
tober.
WILL NOT FIGHT
EXTRADITION
Attorney George A. Knight
Will Defend the Accused
Poisoner.
Mra. W. A. Botkln. the suspected pois
oner, has employed George A. Knight as
attorney. Mr. Knight returned from the
Republican State Convention yesterday
morning and at his office he found a mes
sage from the accused woman, asking
him to take charpe of her case. He im
mediately dispatched a note to Mrs. Bot
kin, informing her that he would call at
the City Prison at the earliest possible
moment. Knight is an old friend of Mrs.
Botkin's familyâ the Browns of Ferndale,
Humboldt County.
The attorney went to the city prison late
yesterday afternoon and had a long con
sultation with his fair client. He was
ushered Into the matron's room, where
Mrs. Botkin is incarcerated, and remained
talking with her nearly two hours. After
leaving Mrs. Botkln Mr. Knight had a
short talk with the prison officials, and
asked them to protect the prisoner from
interviewers and curious Individuals and
to frustrate any scheme to secure a state
ment from her by any underhanded trick.
A peremptory order was immediately
given to the sergeant at the desk, who
books and assigns prisoners to the several
ceils, to obey the request of Mr. Knight
and not place any other female prisoner
who might be arrested In the cell with
the alleged murderess. The Jailer was also
instructed to prevent any outsiders iram,
approaching the door from the corridor
to the room where Mrs. Botkin is con
fined.
When asked what legal steps would be
taken in behalf of Mrs. Botkin her attor
ney said: "Nothing can or will be done
until the Delaware officer arrives in San
Francisco with requisition papers for Mrs.
Botkin. No legal steps to secure her free
dom or release on bonds will be taken for
a few days at least. Mrs. Botkin is in the
hands of the law and is perfectly satisfied
to remain there. She has nothing to fear.
"All I know about the case nas been
obtained from perusing the newspapers,
and as I have been at Sacramento all the
week attending the Republican State Con
vention, I have not followed the story
very closely. However, I am satisfied
that the accused woman Is Innocent of
the charge against her. I have known
her family for years, although I never
met her until to-day. Her parents live
in Ferndale and are highly respected by
all who know them. Her three sisters are
all most estimable women. The parents
are quite well-to-do.
"My client has acted wisely In refusing
to grant interviews to reporters, and I
have advised her to maintain strict si
lence, so far as the newspapers are con
cerned. My reason for silencing my
client is based on the presumption that
the Delaware authorities have what they
consider strong evidence connecting: her
with the tragedy. Were it otherwise
her arrest would not have been ordered.
"Now, I assume it is more than likely
Mrs. Botkin will be taken to Dover for
trial, and as all the newspapers contain
ing references to this case are sent to
the State's attorneys it would be unwise
for the accused to grant any Interviews.
I know, of course, that the newspapers
endeavor to be fair and just In these
cases, but discrepancies and mistakes
will occur and, while not materially af
fecting the result of a trial, they cause
a vast deal of trouble. If the papers
were full of interviews written by differ
ent reporters, although corresponding in
substance, the shading of sentences
would involve the accused In an endless
cross-examination. The prosecuting at
torney would read the statements, note
all inconsistencies 'and demand an expla
nation from the defendant. The publi
cation of all Mrs. Botkin could say in
reference to this terrible double crime
would not incriminate her, but to save
time, trouble and expense for myself and
herself I must forbid any further news
paper interviews.
'"The laws of Delaware are most string
ent and the methods and practices of the
courts diverge widely from ours, and
when I go back there to defend Mrs. Bot
kin. which I shall probably do, I do not
want to become involved In any technical
controversies during the trial, if it ever
takes place,
"There will be no technical fight at this
end. If the requisition papers are all
right Mrs. Botkin will consent to return
with the officer to Delaware. "We will
not begin habeas corpus or any other
proceedings which will delay the speedy
termination of the case.
"Mrs. Botkin has 1 told the police the
story of her life, giving all incidents and
details in full. Nothing she has said can
be construed into evidence against her."
Attorneys McGowan & Squires of this
city will assist Mr. Knight.
Mrs. Botkin's brother-in-law and sister
will arrive here to-day from Ferndale.
They telegraphed to friends Wednesday
to do everything possible for the pris
oner's comfort until they reached here.
t.Tba suspected woman has plenty of
money and her husband gave orders to
a restaurant to supply her meals in tne
City Prison.
Mr. Kotkin returned to Stockton yes
terday afternoon.
HAS REGAINED
HER COMPOSURE
Mrs. Botkin Is the Star Board-
er at the City Prison.
Mrs. Botkin dropped her tragedy queen
airs yesterday and came down to the
realities of everyday life. She addressed
her jailers like twentieth century men and
put aside the hallucination that they were
her vassals and retainers. The luckless
garcon to whom fell the task of bringing
to the prisoner her morning meal got a
gratuitous dose of the emotional wrath
that survived a night spent in the City
Prison.
"Boy," said Mrs. Botkin, with the air
and the deep bass of a barnstorming Lady
Macbeth, "you are five minutes late. Let
it be understood that I am not to be kept
waiting one moment beyond the appointed
time; otherwise I shall have to give my
orders to some other restaurant."
The boy trembled, the lady waved him
aside and then sat down to her breakfast.
Mrs. Botkln has made herself perfectly
at- home, and as comfortable as her
cramped quarters will permit. Yesterday
afternoon she was seen in a white siiK
peignoir, the loose folds of which half dis
played yet half concealed the lines of ncr
rather too plump figure. The sleeves,
which reached only to the shoulder,
showed *to advantage the rounded con
tours of perfectly modeled arms, on which
gleamed bracelets brilliant with precious
stones. She spent the better part of the
day in the matron's sitting-room, and
rocked incesantly in the shabby little
cane-seated chair. Her pose was what
L>elsartian disciples would call "artlessly
negligent," with the white arms encir
cling her head, which was thrown well
over the back of the low-backed chair.
Mrs. Botkin has attained the dignity of
a star at the City Prison. All day long
;l line of morbidly curious women sought
admission to the prisoner. Some came in
sympathy, some to give advice and still
others impelled by the law they rail
"women for women." However, the ma
jority brutally admitted that curiosity
alone brought them there. All were ae
nied admittance.
Mrs. Botkln still adheres to her resolu
tion not to be interviewed. It is her de
clared intention never again to see or
speak with a press representative, and her
Jailers maintain that they have every rea
son to believe she will stand by her word.
The Letter From Dunning.
What shall be done with the letter in
transit from Dunning to Mrs. Botkin on
its arrival here is a problem that is puz
zling the Chief of Police.
The Chief declines to say what action
he will take, but it is supposed that the
solution of tne problem will rest with the
postal authorities.
In ordinary cases a letter could not be
intercepted and opened, but in a case
where the interests of justice would be
subserved, such as the probaonity of giv
ing a clew to a poisoner, it is a question
whether the courts could not be resorted
to in order to have such a letter inter
cepted and opened.
The letter is about due now, and its
final disposition will be watched with the
keenest interest.
COMES FROM A
GOOD FAMILY
Mrs. Botkin's Parents Are
Highly Respected Citi
zens of Ferndale.
HEALDSBURG, Aug. 25.â Mrs. Bot
kin's parents, Colonel Richards Brown
and wife, 76 and 75 years of age respec
tively, have been for twenty years or
more highly respected residents of Fern
dale, Humboldt County. Colonel Brown
is a native of Tennessee and acquired his
military title in the Missouri State mili
tia during the Mexcan War. He was a
pioneer of Nebraska and was the founder
of Brownsville in that State, which was
named in hia honor. He was a banker
and merchant in Brownsville, and was a
member of the Nebraska Territorial Leg
islature. Colonel Brown came to Cali
fornia twenty-three years apo. He is
the father of seven daughters and one
son, Mrs. Botkin being the fourth daugh
ter. The son. A. C. Brown, was at
tached to General Dodges headquarters
at Omaha during the rebellion. Three
of Mrs. Botkin's sisters live with the
old folks in Ferndale, one married sister
lives in New York, one in Kansas City
and her eldest sister, Mrs. McClure, is a
resident of Healdsburg. Mr. and Mrs.
McClure are highly respected residents of
this city and feel deeply the trouble and
notoriety that has been brought upon the
family. Mrs. McOlure returned a few
weeks ago from a visit to her aged par
ents and was met at St. Helena a few
days after her return, on August 4, by
Mrs. Botkin, who had come from San
Francisco, after repeated invitations, to
pay her sister a visit. They drove over
to Healdsburg on Sunday, August 7. and
It was in her sister's home in Healds
burg that Mrs. Uotkin first learned
through the city papers that she was
suspected of being connected with Mrs.
Dnnning's death.

ADVEKTISEMPNTS.
Just now storekeeping is an actual pleasure ; novel-
ties coming in day by day, and everything so different

No Clue at the Postoffice.
The postoffice authorities, in a quiet
way have been attempting to assist the
police in ascertaining in what part of
the city, if at all. the poisoned bonbons
were mailed. Most of the carriers have
been interrogated, but no information of
value has been obtained. It Is pretty
generally conceded by the officers that
the package was mailed at the general
postoffice or at one of the sub-stations.
The box of candy is supposed to have
been put in the mail about August 3 or
4, about the time Mrs. Botkin left San
Francisco for Healdsburg, and had the
package been taken up from any of the
boxes the carrier would doubtless have
some slight recollection of the matter.
Officers have visited many of the drug
stores in the locality where Mrs. Bot
kin roomed, but were unable to obtain
any clews. Druggists keep a record of
the purchases or poison, and thus far,
it is said, no incriminating evidence has
been discovered. Many of Mrs. Botkin's
acquaintances have been questioned by
the police as to whether she had inquired
as to the effects of poison. It Is more
than probable, though, that the person
who so skillfully planned and executed the
horrible murders was cunning enough to

In the vicinity of The Victoria, on Cali

Anxious About Her Mail.
Additional light has been thrown on
Mrs. Botkin's private life at The Victoria
l :omas Kida, a well-educated Japan
?ie, who Is a graduate of a San
soo business college. Speaking of
uter last evening he said:
B. Botkin lived at The Victoria
I was employed there. She had a
stove in her room, on which she
COOlted her meals. Frequently she tipped
m<> to run errands. She received consid
erable mail. Almost every other day a
letter or two would come from Florida
and in these she was particularly inter
ested, and if for any cause they were
not received when expected she would
send me downstairs to the mail box sev
eral times during the day. Once she told
me that the letters were from her cousin
who was one of the crew on the battle
ship New York.
"In a little room adjoining I frequent
ly found empty whisky bottles. She re
ceived very few visitors, one of whom
was a young lady about 25 years old,
medium build and of dark complexion.
When her son, who was about 14 years
old, came to live with her she stopped
receiving company.
"She was absent from the house the
latter part of July for two or three days,
but between July 28 and August 3 or 4
she was in her rooms every day.
"I am positive that I never saw any
candy or candy boxes or poisonous drugs
in her apartments. She was quite tallca
tive but never mentioned anything about
her husband or acquaintances. To my
knowledge she did not make any home
made candy tn her rooms, and she never
gave me any letters or packages to mail."
Kida is an intelligent Japanese, and
readily told his story in good English.
He seemed to entertain a very high re
gard for Mrs. Botkin because, as he said,
she frequently gave htm a quarter for
running errands.
Besieged by the Curious.
Several ineffectual attempts were madÂ©
last "evening 1 to see Mrs. Botkin, and as a
final resort Chief Lees was appealed to.
The Chief positively declined to be a
party to forcing any one into her pres
ence, and Captain Seymour, who is in
charge of the prison, also turned a deaf
ear to all requests of a similar nature.
"Attorney Knight." said the captain,
"gave me positive instructions after his
interview with Mrs. Botkin that on no ac
count should any one be permitted to see
her, and I shall respect his instructions.
Some one awoke Mrs. Botkin about 2
o'clock this morning, demanding to see
her, and both she and her husband com
plained bitterly to the Chief and myself
about it. It will go hard with any one in
the department who permits her to be
annoyed by any outsiders."
Clever Detectives at Work.
Detective Ed Gibson has done the bulk
of the local work on the Dunning case.
He has demonstrated his shrewdness and,
."bility on many mysterious cases, and is
considered one of the best men on the
force. Detective Wren, Gibson's partner,
another able officer, is also detailed on
the case.
Fred Nordholz' Denial.
Fred Nordholz, the clerk In Hadeler's
grocery and liquor store on the southeast
corner of Geary and Larkin streets, says
the interview in The Call wherein he is
quoted on the Dunning case is a mistake.
The reporter got his name confounded
with another clerk.
Want to Hold Primaries.
At a meeting of the Democratic Central
Committee, held last night, the following
resolution was unanimously adopted:
Resolved, That it is the sense of thia club
that primaries be held to determine thp con
stitution of the coming municipal convention
in the city and county of San Francisco, and
that said primaries be .held under the auspices
of the State Central Committee; and it is lur
ther

Carl Spelling, t>r Thielc. Charles Kewel,
James Heal. Charles McCarthy. W, P- Strad
ley and Charles Holcomb is hereby- appointed
to attend upon the new State Central commit
tee at its first meeting and request. said com
mittee to hold primaries In the manner indl
cated in this resolution.
